Logic Belongs to Kalam, Not Just Philosophers
Ghazali refutes the exclusivity claim and identifies logic with Kalam's Kitab al-Nazar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Ghazali affirms the necessity of logic but rejects the philosophers' claim of exclusivity, showing that mutakallimun have their own tradition of rational examination.

Core Idea
Logic is a universal tool, not a property of philosophers.

Ghazali begins with an absurd example to expose the irrationality of extreme rationalism. He then concedes the necessity of logical arts but identifies logic with what mutakallimun call 'Kitab al-Nazar', showing that the philosophers simply rebranded it to intimidate others.

Guided Path
1
Read the pomegranate example
Start by reading the first sentence about the pomegranate. Notice how Ghazali uses a ridiculous requirement to criticize excessive rational demands.
This sets the stage for his argument about logic.
2
Identify Ghazali's concession
Ghazali says the philosophers' statement that logical arts must have their judgments is correct. This shows he does not reject logic itself.
3
Find the alternative names
Ghazali lists several terms used by mutakallimun for logic: Kitab al-Nazar, Kitab al-Jadal, Madarik al-Uqul. Understand that these are equivalent to what philosophers call logic.
Kitab al-Nazar is the primary term in Kalam.
4
Reflect on the intimidation tactic
Ghazali explains that the philosophers changed the name to 'logic' to intimidate weak-minded people into thinking it is a foreign discipline unknown to mutakallimun.
This is a key psychological critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يعرف كون هذه الرمانة حادثة ، ما لم يعرف عدد حباتها وهو هجر من الكلام مستغث عند كل عاقل )
نعم قولهم : إن المنطقيات لابد من أحكامها هو صحيح ، ولكن المنطق ليس مخصوصاً بهم ، وإنما هو الأصل الذي نسميه في فن « الكلام » « كتاب النظر » فغيروا عبارته إلى المنطق تهويلا ، وقد نسميه ( كتاب الجدل » وقد نسميه مدارك العقول » فإذا سمع المتكايس المستضعف ، اسم المنطق ، ظن أنه فن غريب لا يعرفه المتكلمون ، ولا يطلع عليه إلا الفلاسفة ، ونحن لدفع هذا الخيال واستئصال هذه الحيلة فى الإضلال ، نرى أن نفرد القول في « مدارك العقول » في هذا الكتاب ، ونهجر فيه ألفاظ المتكلمين والأصوليين ، بل نوردها بعبارات المنطقيين ، ونصبها في قوالبهم ، ونقتفى آثارهم لفظاً لفظاً ونناظرهم في هذا الكتاب بلغتهم - أعنى بعباراتهم في المنطق - ونوضح أن ما شرطوه في صحة مادة القياس في قسم البرهان من المنطق ، وما شرطوه في صورته في كتاب القياس وما وضعوه من الأوضاع فى « إيساغوجي » و « قاطيغور ياس » التي هي من أجزاء المنطق ومقدماته ، لم يتمكنوا من الوفاء بشيء منه فى علومهم الإلهية .
ولكنا نرى أن نورد ( مدارك العقول » في آخر الكتاب ، فإنه كالآلة لدرك مقصود الكتاب ، ولكن رب ناظر يستغنى عنه في الفهم ، فنؤخره حتى يعرض عنه من لا يحتاج إليه ، ومن لايفهم ألفاظنا في آحاد المسائل في الرد عليهم ، فينبغي أن يبتدئ أولا بحفظ كتاب ( معيار العلم ) الذي هو الملقب بالمنطق عندهم .
And [one] does not know that this pomegranate is originated (haditha) without knowing the number of its seeds, and this is absurd (hajr) speech, rejected by every rational person. Yes, their statement: 'The logical arts must have their judgments' is correct, but logic is not exclusive to them. Rather, it is the principle that we call, in the art of Kalam, 'Kitab al-Nazar' (Book of Examination). So they changed its expression to 'logic' to intimidate. And we sometimes call it 'Kitab al-Jadal' (Book of Dialectics) and sometimes call it 'Madarik al-Uqul' (Perceptions of the Intellects). So when the cunning weak-minded person hears the name 'logic', he thinks it is a strange art that the mutakallimun do not know and that only the philosophers are acquainted with. And we, to repel this illusion and uproot this device in misleading, think that we should devote a discourse to 'Madarik al-Uqul' in this book, and abandon in it the expressions of the mutakallimun and usuliyyun (legal theorists). Rather, we present them in the expressions of the logicians, and pour them into their molds, and follow their traces word for word, and dispute with them in this book in their language – I mean in their expressions in logic – and we clarify that what they stipulated for the validity of the matter (madda) of syllogism in the section of demonstration (burhan) of logic, and what they stipulated for its form (sura) in the book of syllogism (Qiyas), and what they laid down of the conventions in 'Isaghuji' (Isagoge) and 'Qatighuriyas' (Categories), which are parts of logic and its preliminaries, they were unable to fulfill anything of it in their divine sciences (al-ulum al-ilahiyya). But we think that we defer 'Madarik al-Uqul' to the end of the book, for it is like a tool for grasping the purpose of the book. However, some readers may dispense with it for understanding, so we postpone it so that he who does not need it may turn away from it, and he who does not understand our expressions in the individual questions in refuting them should first begin by memorizing the book 'Mi'yar al-Ilm' (The Criterion of Knowledge), which is what they call logic.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is an argument and methodological declaration by Ghazali. He begins by criticizing a hypothetical statement about the necessity of knowing the number of seeds of a pomegranate to know its originatedness—a ridiculous requirement. Then he addresses the philosophers' claim that logical arts are essential. He acknowledges the truth of the necessity of logic but argues that logic is not exclusive to philosophers; rather, it is the same as what mutakallimun call 'Kitab al-Nazar' (Book of Examination). The philosophers merely rebranded it as 'logic' to intimidate. Ghazali then explains that mutakallimun also refer to this discipline as 'Kitab al-Jadal' or 'Madarik al-Uqul'. When a weak-minded person hears 'logic', he is misled into thinking it is foreign to kalam. To counter this deception, Ghazali decides to write a section titled 'Madarik al-Uqul' using the philosophers' own terminology and syllogistic forms, demonstrating that they cannot uphold their own conditions in their divine sciences. He plans to place this section at the end of the book as a tool for understanding the main discussion. However, for those who find it unnecessary or need to understand his arguments in the individual refutations, he recommends first studying his book 'Mi'yar al-Ilm', which is the logical work known as 'Mi'yar al-Ilm' among them.

Key Terms
منطق
كتاب النظر
كتاب الجدل
مدارك العقول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali rejects logic entirely.
Correction: Ghazali affirms the necessity of logic but rejects its monopolization by philosophers.

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali say the mutakallimun call the discipline that the philosophers call 'logic'?
A) Kitab al-Jadal
B) Kitab al-Nazar
C) Madarik al-Uqul
D) Mi'yar al-Ilm

Key Takeaway
Logic is a universal tool known in Kalam as Kitab al-Nazar; philosophers do not own it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What area of knowledge have you dismissed because you associated it with a group or label you distrust? Will you now re-examine it to see if it is actually a valid tool for seeking truth?
?
Ghazali warns that the philosophers changed the name to intimidate. Where in your life have you been intimidated by a name or title, causing you to shy away from learning? What one step will you take this week to overcome that intimidation?
?
You now know that logic is not foreign to Islam but is called Kitab al-Nazar in Kalam. How will you change your approach to studying rational proofs in your faith? Will you actively seek to understand 'Kitab al-Nazar' as part of your learning?

Method of Learning
intermediate
6 min
Ghazali's Strategy: Use Their Own Language
Ghazali outlines his method of using philosophers' own logic to refute them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's methodological decision to adopt the philosophers' logical terminology and argue against them on their own terms to expose their failures.

Core Idea
To defeat the philosophers, Ghazali will use their own language to show their inconsistencies.

Having established that logic is not exclusive to philosophers, Ghazali announces his bold method: he will write a section called 'Madarik al-Uqul' using the logicians' own expressions and syllogistic forms. His goal is to demonstrate that the philosophers themselves cannot fulfill their own stringent conditions in the divine sciences. He also recommends his earlier book 'Mi'yar al-Ilm' for those needing an introduction to logical terminology.

Guided Path
1
Read Ghazali's declaration
Start with 'ونحن لدفع هذا الخيال' and read through to the end. Ghazali clearly states his plan to use the philosophers' language.
Notice his phrase 'ونهجر فيه ألفاظ المتكلمين' – he abandons kalam terminology.
2
Identify the logical terms and books mentioned
Ghazali mentions 'إيساغوجي' (Isagoge), 'قاطيغورياس' (Categories), 'معيار العلم' (Mi'yar al-Ilm). These are standard logical works. Understand that he is well-versed in their system.
3
Understand his challenge to philosophers
Ghazali claims that the philosophers cannot fulfill their own conditions for valid syllogisms in their divine sciences (إلهيات). This is the core of his refutation.
He says 'لم يتمكنوا من الوفاء بشيء منه' – they could not fulfill anything of it.
4
Note the placement of this section
Ghazali plans to defer 'Madarik al-Uqul' to the end of the book, as a tool for those who need it. He also advises studying 'Mi'yar al-Ilm' first for beginners.
This shows his pedagogical awareness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

What does Ghazali recommend for those who do not understand his logical expressions in the individual refutations?
A) Read 'Kitab al-Nazar'

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you argue about religious truth, do you insist on your own terminology or adapt to your opponent's language? How might adopting Ghazali's strategy change the effectiveness of your da'wah? Be specific about a recent discussion.
?
Ghazali placed his logical tool at the end for those who need it. Are you willing to set aside your pride and revisit foundational knowledge (like logic or Arabic) to strengthen your understanding? What will you do this week to address a gap?
?
Are there areas in your life where you rely on technical jargon (e.g., Islamic terms like 'tawheed', 'shirk') that you use without fully understanding their deeper implications? How can you ensure you are not just repeating words but truly grasping concepts as Ghazali demands?

The Twenty Questions of the Tahafut
Imam Ghazali lists the twenty problems he will refute in the Tahafut.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure and scope of Ghazali's critique in the Tahafut al-Falasifa as outlined in his list of twenty questions.

Core Idea
Ghazali's twenty questions systematically dismantle philosophy's core claims.

Before diving into his arguments, Imam Ghazali provides a roadmap of the twenty problems he will refute. This list reveals the full scope of his critique, spanning metaphysics, natural philosophy, and theology. It shows how he targets both the foundations and the details of the philosophers' worldview.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the numbered list from 1 to 20.
This is a table of contents, so each item will be expanded later in the book.
2
Identify thematic clusters
Group the questions by topic: eternity of the world (1-2), God's nature and attributes (3-10), God's knowledge (11-13), heavens (14-16), miracles (17), soul (18-19), resurrection (20).
This helps see the logical progression from metaphysics to eschatology.
3
Reflect on the order
Consider why Ghazali starts with the eternity of the world and ends with bodily resurrection. The order builds from foundational cosmological issues to specific theological doctrines.
The list shows a deliberate move from outer cosmos to inner soul to final destiny.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنذكر الآن بعد المقدمات فهرست المسائل التي أظهرنا تناقض مذهبهم فيها في هذا الكتاب وهي عشرون مسألة: المسألة الأولى: إبطال مذهبهم في أزلية العالم. الثانية: إبطال مذهبهم في أبدية العالم. الثالثة: بيان تلبيسهم في قولهم: إن الله صانع العالم، وإن العالم صنعه. الرابعة: في تعجيزهم عن إثبات الصانع. الخامسة: في تعجيزهم عن إقامة الدليل على استحالة إلهين في العالم. السادسة: في إبطال مذهبهم في نفي الصفات. السابعة: في إبطال قولهم: إن ذات الأول لا تنقسم بالجنس والفصل. الثامنة: في إبطال قولهم: إن الأول موجود بسيط بلا ماهية. التاسعة: في تعجيزهم عن بيان أن الأول ليس بجسم. العاشرة: في بيان أن القول بالدهر ونفي الصانع لازم لهم. الحادية عشرة: في تعجيزهم عن القول بأن الأول يعلم غيره. الثانية عشرة: في تعجيزهم عن القول بأنه يعلم ذاته. الثالثة عشرة: في إبطال قولهم: إن الأول لا يعلم الجزئيات. الرابعة عشرة: في قولهم: إن السماء حيوان متحرك بالإرادة. الخامسة عشرة: في إبطال ما ذكروه من الغرض المحرك للسماء. السادسة عشرة: في إبطال قولهم: إن نفوس السماوات تعلم جميع الجزئيات. السابعة عشرة: في إبطال قولهم باستحالة خرق العادات. الثامنة عشرة: في قولهم: إن نفس الإنسان جوهر قائم بنفسه ليس بجسم ولا عرض. التاسعة عشرة: قولهم باستحالة الفناء على النفوس البشرية. العشرون: في إبطال إنكارهم لبعث الأجساد مع التلذذ والتألم في الجنة والنار باللذات والآلام الجسمانية. فهذا ما أردنا أن نذكر تناقضهم فيه من جملة علومهم الإلهية والطبيعية.
And we will now mention, after the introductions, an index of the questions in which we have shown the contradiction of their doctrine in this book, and they are twenty questions: The first question: Refuting their doctrine of the pre-eternity of the world. The second: Refuting their doctrine of the post-eternity of the world. The third: Exposing their deception in their statement that God is the maker of the world, and that the world is His making. The fourth: Regarding incapacitating them from proving the Maker. The fifth: Regarding incapacitating them from establishing the proof of the impossibility of two gods in the world. The sixth: Regarding refuting their doctrine of denying the attributes. The seventh: Regarding refuting their statement that the essence of the First is not divisible by genus and differentia. The eighth: Regarding refuting their statement that the First is a simple existent without quiddity. The ninth: Regarding incapacitating them from explaining that the First is not a body. The tenth: Regarding explaining that the doctrine of time (dahr) and the denial of the Maker is necessarily incumbent upon them. The eleventh: Regarding incapacitating them from the statement that the First knows other than Himself. The twelfth: Regarding incapacitating them from the statement that He knows His own essence. The thirteenth: Regarding refuting their statement that the First does not know particulars. The fourteenth: Regarding their statement that the heaven is an animal moving by will. The fifteenth: Regarding refuting what they mentioned of the purpose moving the heaven. The sixteenth: Regarding refuting their statement that the souls of the heavens know all particulars. The seventeenth: Regarding refuting their statement of the impossibility of breaking habits (miracles). The eighteenth: Regarding their statement that the human soul is a self-subsisting substance, not a body nor an accident. The nineteenth: Their statement of the impossibility of annihilation upon human souls. The twentieth: Regarding refuting their denial of the resurrection of bodies with pleasure and pain in Paradise and Hell by physical pleasures and pains. This is what we intended to mention of their contradiction from the totality of their divine and natural sciences.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a table of contents listing the twenty topics Imam Ghazali will address in the Tahafut al-Falasifa. He begins by stating that after the introductions, he will present an index of the questions where he has shown the contradiction of the philosophers' doctrine. He then enumerates them: questions 1-2 deal with the eternity of the world (pre-eternity and post-eternity), question 3 exposes their deception about God as maker, questions 4-5 challenge their ability to prove the Maker and the impossibility of two gods, question 6 attacks their denial of divine attributes, questions 7-8 address their claims about the simplicity and quiddity of the First (God), question 9 challenges their proof that the First is not a body, question 10 argues that their view of time (dahr) implies atheism, questions 11-13 discuss God's knowledge (of others and Himself, and particulars), questions 14-16 critique their views on the heavens (as animal, its purpose, and the souls of heavens), question 17 concerns the possibility of miracles (breaking habits), question 18 discusses the nature of the human soul, question 19 argues against the immortality of the soul (since they claim annihilation is impossible, Ghazali refutes that in favor of bodily resurrection), and question 20 refutes their denial of bodily resurrection with physical pleasures and pains. He concludes by saying that these are the issues from their divine and natural sciences.

Key Terms
أزلية العالم
أبدية العالم
الصانع
الصفات
الجنس والفصل
ماهية
الدهر
الجزئيات
خرق العادات
نفس الإنسان
جوهر قائم بنفسه
الفناء
بعث الأجساد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ghazali rejects all philosophy outright.
Correction: Ghazali's twenty questions show he targets specific contradictions in the philosophers' doctrines, not philosophy as a whole. He engages with their arguments and refutes them point by point.

Checkpoint
According to the list, which question number discusses the nature of the human soul?
A) 17
B) 18
C) 19
D) 20

Key Takeaway
The twenty questions form the backbone of Ghazali's critique, targeting both the method and conclusions of the philosophers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your own worldview, are there beliefs you hold that contradict each other? Will you honestly list them today and seek the truth, even if it unsettles you?
?
If the most basic question—whether the world is eternal or created—can be debated, what other foundational beliefs have you accepted without proof? Are you willing to investigate them with the same rigor as al-Ghazali?
?
Al-Ghazali did not dismiss the philosophers wholesale; he engaged their arguments and exposed contradictions. In your own life, do you reject ideas without understanding them? Whose ideas are you willing to struggle with for the sake of truth, even if it takes effort?

Method of Learning

3 min
Mathematics: A Valid Science
Ghazali affirms the validity of mathematics as a science.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali affirms the validity of mathematics and distinguishes it from the errors of metaphysics.

Core Idea
Mathematics, based on arithmetic and geometry, is sound and not to be denied.

In this brief passage from the Tahafut, Ghazali clarifies that his critique of philosophy does not extend to all its branches. He explicitly states that mathematics, which reduces to arithmetic and geometry, is not subject to denial or opposition. This lesson explores his reasoning and its implications for the wider debate.

Guided Path
1
Read Ghazali’s statement on mathematics
Read the Arabic sentence and its translation carefully. Notice the directness of his affirmation.
The Arabic uses 'لا معنى لإنكارها' – 'no meaning to denying them'.
2
Understand why it is not to be denied
Note his reasoning: 'for they reduce to arithmetic and geometry'. This implies that mathematics is based on evident foundations.
3
Consider the significance
This shows Ghazali's critique is selective. He accepts mathematics as a valid science, unlike certain metaphysical claims.
This distinguishes his position from a wholesale rejection of philosophy.

This passage is a brief aside where Ghazali clarifies his stance on two branches of philosophy that he does not attack. Ghazali defines logic as the instrument of thought and minimizes its disagreements.

The Exclusion of Mathematics and Logic from Refutation
Ghazali clarifies the scope of his critique, excluding mathematics and logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Imam al-Ghazali's critique of the philosophers in the Tahafut is limited to their metaphysical and natural claims, explicitly excluding mathematics and logic as certain and uncontroversial sciences.

Core Idea
Ghazali targets metaphysics and physics, not math or logic.

Imam al-Ghazali's Tahafut al-Falasifa is a powerful critique of philosophical doctrines, but it is not a wholesale rejection of all rational inquiry. In this passage, he sets clear boundaries: his exposure of contradictions applies only to the divine and natural sciences, while mathematics and logic are left untouched. This methodological clarification is essential for understanding the precise scope of his project and avoiding the common misconception that he opposed all forms of reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the clear demarcation Ghazali makes between different sciences.
Pay attention to the terms ilāhiyyāt (divine sciences) and ṭabīʿiyyāt (natural sciences) versus riyāḍiyyāt (mathematics) and manṭiqiyyāt (logic).
2
Identify the two excluded sciences
Identify the two fields Ghazali explicitly excludes from his critique. What reasons does he give for each?
3
Understand the reason for exclusion
Reflect on why mathematics and logic are considered certain or uncontroversial. How does this relate to Ghazali's broader purpose?
Mathematics is based on calculation and geometry, logic is the instrument of thought—both are tools, not speculative doctrines.
4
Connect to the Miʿyār al-ʿIlm
Ghazali refers the reader to his work 'The Standard of Knowledge' for necessary logical tools. Think about how this shows his positive engagement with logic.
This demonstrates that Ghazali valued logic as a tool, even while critiquing philosophical conclusions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذا ما أردنا أن نذكر تناقضهم فيه من جملة علومهم الإلهية والطبيعية، وأما الرياضيات فلا معنى لإنكارها ولا للمخالفة فيها، فإنها ترجع إلى الحساب والهندسة.
وأما المنطقيات فهى نظر فى آلة الفكر فى المعقولات، ولا يتفق فيه خلاف به مبالاة، وسنورد في كتاب « معيار العلم » من جملته ما يحتاج إليه لفهم مضمون هذا الكتاب إن شاء الله
This is what we intended to mention of their contradictions from among their divine and natural sciences. As for the mathematical sciences, there is no meaning to denying them or opposing them, for they go back to calculation and geometry. And as for the logical sciences, they are an investigation into the instrument of thought concerning intelligibles, and there is no disagreement about them that matters, and we will bring in the book 'The Standard of Knowledge' from its entirety what is needed to understand the content of this book, God willing.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is a concluding remark from the author, Imam al-Ghazali, where he clarifies the scope of his critique in the Tahafut. He states that his exposure of contradictions among the philosophers pertains to their divine and natural sciences (ilāhiyyāt wa ṭabīʿiyyāt). He then explicitly excludes the mathematical sciences (riyāḍiyyāt) from this critique, saying there is no point in denying them or opposing them because they are based on calculation and geometry, implying they are certain and not subject to the same contradictions. Similarly, he excludes the logical sciences (manṭiqiyyāt), describing them as an investigation into the instrument of thought concerning intelligibles (ālat al-fikr fī al-maʿqūlāt) and stating that there is no significant disagreement about them. He further mentions his intention to present in his book 'Miʿyār al-ʿIlm' (The Standard of Knowledge) the parts of logic needed to understand the content of this book (the Tahafut). Thus, Ghazali limits his attack to the philosophical claims that conflict with Islamic teachings, while acknowledging the utility and non-controversial nature of mathematics and logic.

Key Terms
الرياضيات (riyāḍiyyāt)
المنطقيات (manṭiqiyyāt)
الآلهيات (ilāhiyyāt)
الطبيعيات (ṭabīʿiyyāt)
معيار العلم (Miʿyār al-ʿIlm)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Ghazali rejects all philosophy or rational inquiry.
Correction: Ghazali explicitly excludes mathematics and logic from his critique, showing he accepts certain sciences as certain and useful tools.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, which branches of philosophy does Ghazali consider not subject to his critique?
A) Divine sciences and natural sciences
B) Mathematical sciences and logical sciences
C) Only the natural sciences
D) All philosophical sciences

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's critique in the Tahafut targets only the divine and natural sciences, not mathematics or logic, which he regards as certain and unproblematic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you come across a critique of a certain worldview, do you tend to dismiss the entire system or do you carefully separate what is being rejected from what is accepted? How can Ghazali's precise distinction between refuted and non-refuted sciences improve your own approach to evaluating ideas?
?
Ghazali acknowledges mathematics as certain and indisputable. Is there any knowledge in your own life that you hold with similar certainty? How does that certainty shape your actions and decisions?
?
Ghazali promises to provide the necessary logical tools in another book. Are you willing to learn tools (like logic) from sources you may have reservations about? What might that process require from you in terms of humility and openness?

Aquinas's Third Position on Temporality and Eternity
Comparison of Aquinas, Ibn Sina, and al-Ghazali on temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Aquinas's unique position that both temporality and eternity are rationally possible, and why he refers the matter to revelation.

Core Idea
Aquinas proposes a third way: both eternity and temporality are possible; revelation decides.

This concluding passage compares three major thinkers on the world's origin. Ibn Sina argues the world is necessarily eternal, al-Ghazali argues it is necessarily created in time. Aquinas takes a middle path, affirming both as rationally possible and leaving the final decision to revealed scripture. The author warns against using weak philosophical proofs for religious doctrines.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal English translation. Notice the three distinct positions presented.
The passage is short but dense; take your time.
2
Identify the three positions
Find where Aquinas's view is stated, then Ibn Sina's, then al-Ghazali's. Note the key Arabic terms: الحدوث (temporality), القدم (eternity), الوحي (revelation).
3
Understand Aquinas's reasoning
Aquinas says both are possible, so the matter should be referred to revelation. Ask: Why does he think philosophical proofs are insufficient?
The passage warns that using weak proofs can weaken religious doctrine.
4
Reflect on the broader implication
Consider the author's point that Aquinas's view is similar to al-Ghazali and Descartes. Think about the relationship between faith and reason in this discussion.
This connects to the introduction mentioned in the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تلكم هي حكاية القديس توما للمشكلة، وذلكم هو رأيه بصددها، وهو رأى يختلف عما لابن سينا والغزالى. إنه يجعل الحدوث والقدم ممكنين، أما ابن سينا فإنه يجعل الحدوث مستحيلا، وأما الغزالي فيجعله واجباً. وبهذا نجد أنه قد تولد عندنا رأى ثالث في المسألة. ومن رأى القديس توما الرجوع بمسألة الحدوث إلى الوحي ونصوصه؛ أما محاولة استمدادها من هذه الحجج القابلة للنقاش والنقد؛ فإن فيه - مع إضعاف فكرة الحدوث - إضعافاً للعقيدة الدينية جملة إذ يظن بها أنها مستقاة من مثل هذه الحجج. وهو رأى شبيه بما سجلناه - في المقدمة - للغزالى وديكارت.
This is Saint Thomas's account of the problem, and this is his opinion concerning it, and it is an opinion different from that of Ibn Sina and al-Ghazali. He makes temporality and eternity both possible, while Ibn Sina makes temporality impossible, and al-Ghazali makes it necessary. And by this we find that a third opinion has been generated regarding the issue. And Saint Thomas's opinion is to refer the question of temporality to revelation and its texts; as for the attempt to derive it from these arguable and criticisable proofs; it involves – along with weakening the idea of temporality – a weakening of the religious doctrine as a whole, since it would be thought that it is derived from such arguments. And it is an opinion similar to what we recorded – in the introduction – for al-Ghazali and Descartes.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This concluding passage summarizes Aquinas's unique position. Unlike Ibn Sina, who held that temporality is impossible (the world is eternal), and al-Ghazali, who held that temporality is necessary (the world is created in time), Aquinas makes both possibilities rationally valid. He thus proposes a third opinion: the matter is to be referred to revelation, not to philosophical proof. Attempting to prove temporality by weak arguments undermines both the doctrine and religion in general. The author notes this is similar to the views of al-Ghazali and Descartes mentioned in the introduction.

Key Terms
الحدوث
القدم
الوحي
العقيدة الدينية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Aquinas denies both eternity and temporality, or that he takes a side like Ibn Sina or al-Ghazali.
Correction: Aquinas affirms both as rationally possible and leaves the final decision to revelation, not to philosophical proof.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the key difference between Aquinas's view and that of Ibn Sina?
A) Aquinas says temporality is impossible, Ibn Sina says it is possible
B) Aquinas says both are possible, Ibn Sina says temporality is impossible
C) Aquinas says the world is eternal, Ibn Sina says it is temporal
D) Aquinas says eternity is impossible, Ibn Sina says it is necessary

Key Takeaway
Aquinas's position is that reason alone cannot decide whether the world is eternal or temporal; the matter must be settled by revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you defend your faith to others, do you rely more on rational arguments or on the texts of revelation? Which has given you greater certainty, and why?
?
The lesson warns that using weak arguments for religious doctrines weakens the entire faith. Have you ever used an argument you later realized was flawed? What effect did that have on your own conviction, and how did you respond?
?
Are you willing to let revelation settle a question that your reason cannot definitively answer? Name one area in your life where you need to submit to revelation despite intellectual uncertainty, and commit to doing so today.

Aquinas on Faith, Reason, and Temporal Creation
Aquinas distinguishes faith and reason on temporal creation, warns against weak proofs, and presents a third position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Thomas Aquinas's position on temporal creation, why he distinguishes between faith and reason, and how his view differs from Ibn Sina and al-Ghazali.

Core Idea
For Aquinas, temporal creation is a matter of faith, not rational proof.

In this passage, we encounter a third major perspective on the question of whether the world is created in time or eternal. Thomas Aquinas offers a nuanced view that avoids both the rationalist certainty of Ibn Sina and the theological necessity of al-Ghazali. His approach prioritizes revelation over reason, while warning that weak rational arguments can actually harm religious belief.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp Aquinas's argument in his own words.
The passage is a commentary on Aquinas's view, not his own writing.
2
Identify Aquinas's main claim
Find the sentence where Aquinas states how we can know temporal creation. Notice he says it is known only by faith, not by rational demonstration.
3
Understand his warning
Aquinas warns against using weak arguments to support beliefs. Why does he think this harms faith? Look for the phrase about 'arguments that are not decisive'.
This is a methodological point: weak proofs can discredit religion.
4
Compare the three positions
The passage contrasts Aquinas's view with Ibn Sina and al-Ghazali. List how each thinker evaluates the possibility of temporal creation (ḥudūth).
Ibn Sina: impossible; al-Ghazali: necessary; Aquinas: possible but known by faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويخرج القديس توما من هذه المناقشة بأن الحدوث لا يعلم إلا بالإيمان، وبأن في اعتبار ذلك فائدة لمن يدعى إثبات العقائد بالبرهان ، فلا يأتى بحجج غير قاطعة لئلا يظن بنا أننا إنما نتمسك بالعقائد استناداً إلى مثل هذه الحجج فما أظهر التمييز هذا بين الإيمان والعقل ! ] . تاريخ الفلسفة الأوربية في العصر الوسيط يوجد ص ۱۸۲ ، ۳ ، ، دار الكاتب المصرى تلكم هي حكاية القديس توما للمشكلة ، وذلكم هو رأيه بصددها ، وهو رأى يختلف عما لابن سينا والغزالى . إنه يجعل الحدوث والقدم ممكنين ، أما ابن سينا فإنه يجعل الحدوث مستحيلا ، وأما الغزالي فيجعله واجباً . و بهذا نجد أنه قد تولد عندنا رأى ثالث في المسألة ومن رأى القديس توما الرجوع بمسألة الحدوث إلى الأمين ونصوصه ؛ أما محاولة استمدادها من هذه الحجج القابلة للنقاش والنقد ؛ فإن فيه - مع إضعاف فكرة الحدوث - إضعافاً للعقيدة الدينية جملة إذ يظن بها أنها مستقاة من مثل هذه الحجج. وهو رأى شبيه بما سجلناه - في المقدمة - للغزالي وديكارت
And Thomas Aquinas emerges from this discussion by [saying] that temporal creation is not known except by faith, and that considering that there is a benefit for those who claim to establish beliefs by demonstration, so they should not bring arguments that are not decisive, lest it be thought that we only adhere to beliefs based on such arguments. How clear this distinction between faith and reason is! ... That is Aquinas's account of the problem and that is his opinion regarding it. It is an opinion that differs from that of Ibn Sina and al-Ghazali. He makes ḥudūth and qidam both possible, whereas Ibn Sina makes ḥudūth impossible, and al-Ghazali makes it necessary. And thus we find that a third opinion has emerged for us in this issue. And it is from Aquinas's opinion to refer the issue of temporal creation to revelation and its texts. As for the attempt to derive it from these arguments that are open to discussion and criticism, that entails—alongside weakening the idea of temporal creation—a weakening of religious belief altogether, because it is thought that it is derived from such arguments. And it is an opinion similar to what we recorded—in the introduction—for al-Ghazali and Descartes.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage presents the view of Thomas Aquinas on the question of the temporal creation of the world. Aquinas distinguishes between faith and reason: temporal creation cannot be known by rational proof but only by faith. He warns against using weak arguments to defend religious beliefs, as this may give the impression that religion depends on such shaky reasoning. Aquinas holds that both temporal creation and pre-eternity are rationally possible, whereas Ibn Sina considered temporal creation impossible because an eternal effect must follow from an eternal cause, and al-Ghazali considered it necessary as a matter of divine will. Aquinas's approach is to refer the matter to revelation (the 'trustworthy texts'), rather than to rational arguments. The passage notes that this third opinion resembles what Ghazali and Descartes also held, as mentioned in the introduction.

Key Terms
حدوث (ḥudūth)
قدم (qidam)
إيمان (īmān)
برهان (burhān)
نصوص (nuṣūṣ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the issue of temporal creation must be settled by reason alone.
Correction: Aquinas argues that temporal creation is known only by faith, and that reason alone cannot decide between temporal creation and pre-eternity.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is Thomas Aquinas's opinion on the possibility of temporal creation?
A) It is necessary, following al-Ghazali.
B) It is impossible, following Ibn Sina.
C) It is possible but known only by faith.
D) It is possible and knowable by reason.

Key Takeaway
Aquinas holds that temporal creation is a matter of faith, not reason, and that both temporal creation and pre-eternity are rationally possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there areas of your faith where you rely on weak intellectual arguments that, if undermined, could shake your belief? What would it mean for you to base your faith solely on revelation, even when your reason cannot fully prove it?
?
When you encounter a theological question that reason cannot decisively answer, do you feel unsettled? How does Aquinas's confidence in revelation challenge you to trust God's word over your own understanding?
?
Think of a time when you used a weak argument to defend your beliefs. How did that affect your own certainty? What would it look like for you to humbly admit that some things are known only by faith?

Ghazali's Method in the Tahafut: Destructive Criticism
Ghazali responds to an objection and explains his method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's purpose in writing the Tahafut: to expose the inconsistency of the philosophers through counterarguments, without aiming to prove Islamic doctrine.

Core Idea
The Tahafut destroys; Qawa'id al-'Aqa'id builds.

In this passage, Ghazali addresses a key criticism of his method: that he merely counters one problem with another without offering solutions. He clarifies that his intent is not to resolve philosophical problems but to show their inherent contradictions. This lesson establishes the precise scope of the Tahafut and its relationship to his later constructive work.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the objection-response structure and Ghazali's defensive tone.
2
Identify Ghazali's stated purpose
Ghazali says he only aims to 'muddy their doctrine' and show their 'incoherence' (tahafut). Find these phrases in the text.
3
Distinguish the two works
Ghazali contrasts the Tahafut with his upcoming book 'Qawa'id al-'Aqa'id'. What is the difference in their aims?
One is destructive (hadm), the other constructive (ithbat).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: فقد عولتم في جميع الاعتراضات على مقابلة الإشكالات بالإشكالات، ولم تحلوا ما أوردوه من الإشكالات قلنا: المعارضة تبين فساد الكلام لا محالة، وينحل وجه الإشكال في تقدير المعارضة والمطالبة، ونحن لم نلتزم في هذا الكتاب إلا تكدير مذهبهم، والتغيير في وجوه أدلتهم بما يبين تهافتهم، ولم نتطرق للذب عن مذهب معين، فلم يخرج عن مقصود الكتاب، ولا نستقصى القول في الأدلة الدالة على حدوث العالم، إذ غرضنا إبطال دعواهم معرفة القدم، وأما إثبات المذهب الحق فسنصف فيه كتاباً بعد الفراغ من هذا، إن ساعد التوفيق إن شاء الله تعالى ونسميه (قواعد العقائد) ونعتنى فيه بالإثبات كما اعتنينا في هذا الكتاب بالهدم، والله أعلم.
If it is said: 'You have relied in all your objections on confronting problems with problems, and you have not resolved the problems they raised,' we say: The counterargument inevitably shows the corruption of the speech, and the aspect of the problem is resolved by considering the counterargument and the demand. We have not undertaken in this book anything except to muddy their doctrine and to alter the faces of their proofs in a way that shows their incoherence. We did not set out to defend a specific doctrine. So it has not gone beyond the purpose of the book. And we do not exhaustively discuss the proofs for the temporal origination of the world, for our aim is to invalidate their claim to know eternity. As for the proof of the true doctrine, we will describe a book on it after this, if success grants, God willing, and we will call it 'Foundations of Creeds' (Qawa'id al-'Aqa'id). We will take care in it to establish as we have taken care in this book to destroy. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage clarifies the methodology of the Tahafut. Ghazali responds to a potential objection that he only counters one problem with another without solving anything. He explains that the purpose is to show the corruption of the philosophers' arguments through counterargument (mu'arada). The Tahafut is not a work of positive theology; it is destructive criticism. Ghazali explicitly states that he does not aim to prove the temporal origination of the world (huduth) here, but only to refute the philosophers' claim to have proven eternity. He promises a separate work, 'Qawa'id al-'Aqa'id', where he will systematically establish the true Islamic doctrine. This passage is crucial for understanding the scope and intention of the Tahafut.

Key Terms
معارضة (mu'arada)
إشكال (ishkal)
تهافت (tahafut)
هدم (hadm)
إثبات (ithbat)
قواعد العقائد (Qawa'id al-'Aqa'id)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Tahafut is meant to provide proofs for Islamic beliefs such as the temporal origination of the world.
Correction: Ghazali explicitly states that his aim is only to refute the philosophers' claims, not to establish positive doctrine. He reserves construction for a later work.

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali say is the main purpose of the Tahafut?
A) To prove the existence of God.
B) To show the incoherence of the philosophers.
C) To defend a specific school of kalam.
D) To explain the temporal origination of the world.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's Tahafut is purely destructive; it exposes the inconsistency of the philosophers without claiming to establish the truth, which he promises to do in a later work.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever criticized someone's belief or argument without having a clear positive truth to offer in its place? How does Ghazali's separation of destruction and construction challenge you to examine your own motivations when you debate or correct others? What will you do differently the next time you engage in a disagreement?
?
Ghazali promises to write a book of construction after finishing the Tahafut. Are you more inclined to tear down or to build up in your own spiritual life? Identify one specific area where you have been merely critical without putting effort into establishing a sound foundation, and commit to taking a step toward construction this week.
?
The passage ends with 'And God knows best' (Wallahu a'lam). Do you approach intellectual discussions with this same sense of humility, knowing that your refutations are not the final word? How can you cultivate that humility today, especially when you are convinced you are right?

Ghazali's Method: Refutation through Counter-Arguments
Ghazali responds to criticism of his method and states his purpose.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's dialectical method of counter-argument (muʿāraḍa) and how he distinguishes between demolition (hadm) and affirmation (ithbāt) across his works.

Core Idea
Ghazali's Tahafut is a demolition tool, not a blueprint of his own beliefs.

Ghazali's Tahafut al-Falasifa is often misunderstood. In this passage, he directly addresses the charge that he merely substitutes one problem for another. He responds by clarifying his method: counter-argument reveals the inconsistency of the philosophers' claims, and his purpose is not to establish truth but to show their failure. This lesson unpacks his response and its implications for reading the Tahafut.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objector charges Ghazali with merely countering problems with problems without solving them. Understand this criticism fully before reading Ghazali's answer.
2
Reflect on Ghazali's answer
Ghazali asserts that counter-argument (muʿāraḍa) inevitably shows the corruption of the opponent's position, resolving the problem in the process.
He does not claim to solve all problems, only to expose inconsistency.
3
Identify the book's purpose
He explicitly states his aim is to 'muddy their doctrine and alter the face of their evidences' to show their inconsistency (tahāfut). He does not commit to defending a specific doctrine.
This is the key to understanding the dialectical nature of the Tahafut.
4
Note the future work
Ghazali announces a separate book, Qawāʿid al-ʿAqāʾid, which will focus on positive doctrine (ithbāt) just as this book focuses on demolition (hadm).
This shows planning and methodological consistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: فقد عولتم في جميع الاعتراضات على مقابلة الإشكالات بالإشكالات، ولم تحلوا ما أوردوه من الإشكالات؟ قلنا: المعارضة تبين فساد الكلام لا محالة، وينحل وجه الإشكال في تقدير المعارضة والمطالبة، ونحن لم نلتزم في هذا الكتاب إلا تكدير مذهبهم والتغيير في وجوه أدلتهم بما يبين تهافتهم، ولم نتطرق للذب عن مذهب معين، فلم مقصود الكتاب، ولا نستقصي القول في الأدلة الدالة على [؟] نخرج الحدوث، إذ غرضنا إبطال دعواهم معرفة القدم، وأما إثبات المذهب الحق فسنصف فيه كتاباً بعد الفراغ من هذا، إن ساعد التوفيق إن شاء الله تعالى، ونسميه قواعد العقائد، ونعتنى فيه بالإثبات كما اعتنينا في هذا الكتاب بالهدم، والله أعلم.
If it is said: 'You have relied in all your objections on countering problems with problems, and you have not solved the problems they raised?' We say: Counter-argument reveals the corruption of the speech inevitably, and the face of the problem is resolved in the estimation of the counter-argument and the demand. And we have not undertaken in this book except to muddy their doctrine and to change the aspects of their evidences in a way that shows their inconsistency (tahāfut). And we have not set out to defend a particular doctrine. For this is the purpose of the book. And we do not exhaustively discuss the evidences that indicate [؟] we derive temporality (ḥudūth), for our aim is to refute their claim of knowing pre-eternity (qidam). As for establishing the true doctrine, we will write a book about it after finishing this one, if success aids, God willing, and we will call it 'Qawāʿid al-ʿAqāʾid' (Foundations of Beliefs). And we will concern ourselves in it with affirmation (ithbāt) just as we have concerned ourselves in this book with demolition (hadm). And God knows best.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector charges Ghazali with merely countering problems with problems without solving them. Ghazali responds that counter-argument (muʿāraḍa) suffices to show the corruption of the philosophers' position, which is his aim. He explains that he never committed to defending a specific doctrine; his only purpose in this book is to 'muddle their doctrine and alter the face of their evidences' to expose their inconsistency (tahāfut). He further clarifies that his goal is not to prove the world's temporality exhaustively, but rather to refute their claim to know pre-eternity. For positive doctrine, he announces a future book 'Qawāʿid al-ʿAqāʾid' that will focus on affirmation (ithbāt) as this book focuses on demolition (hadm). This methodological note is crucial for understanding the dialectical nature of the Tahāfut.

Key Terms
معارضة
تهافت
هدم
إثبات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ghazali's Tahafut represents his own positive theological position.
Correction: Ghazali explicitly states he does not defend a particular doctrine in this book; his aim is only to expose the inconsistency of the philosophers' arguments. His positive views are reserved for a separate work.

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali say is the purpose of his book Tahafut al-Falasifa?
A) To prove the falsity of philosophy conclusively.
B) To demonstrate the inconsistency of the philosophers' arguments.
C) To establish the true Islamic doctrine.
D) To solve all problems raised by the philosophers.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's Tahafut is a work of demolition, not construction; its aim is to expose inconsistency, not to prove truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you argue against a false belief, do you ever leave the other person more confused than before, without offering a positive alternative? How often do you follow your critiques with constructive teaching?
?
Ghazali separated his Tahafut (demolition) from his Qawāʿid al-ʿAqāʾid (affirmation). In your own life, are you spending all your energy refuting others while your own foundations remain unbuilt? What is the 'positive work' you have been delaying?
?
The objector accused Ghazali of merely countering problems with problems. Do you ever use the same tactic—pointing out flaws in others' views without ever building your own? How would your faith change if you treated refutation only as a first step toward building truth?

Galen's Argument and Ghazali's First Objection
Galen's proof and Ghazali's logical objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Galen's argument for the sun's incorruptibility and Ghazali's first objection regarding the logical structure of conditional syllogisms.

Core Idea
A corrupt thing need not wither; sudden corruption is possible.

Galen argued that constant observations prove the sun never fades, so it must be eternal. But Ghazali points out a flaw: the logic linking corruption to withering is not necessary. Let's examine the argument and the first objection, which targets the form of the conditional syllogism itself.

Guided Path
1
Read Galen's original proof
Read the first part of the passage where Galen's argument is presented: 'If the sun were susceptible to annihilation, then over a long period withering would appear...'. Understand the logical flow.
2
Identify the conditional syllogism
Notice that Galen's argument is a conditional conjunctive syllogism (sharṭī muttaṣil). The structure is: If P then Q; not Q; therefore not P.
Ghazali refers to this as 'الشرطى المتصل'.
3
Understand Ghazali's first objection
Ghazali argues that the consequent (withering) does not necessarily follow from the antecedent (corruption). He says corruption could occur suddenly without any prior withering, so the conditional is false unless one adds the extra condition that corruption must be of the withering type.
This shows Galen's major premise is not universally accepted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول ما تمسك به جالينوس إذ قال : لو كانت الشمس مثلا تقبل الانعدام ، لظهر فيها ذبول فى مدة مديدة ، والأرصاد الدالة على مقدارها منذ آلاف السنين لا تدل إلا على هذا المقدار ، فلما لم تذبل في هذه الآماد الطويلة ، دل على أنها لا تفسد . الاعتراض عليه من وجوه . الأول : أن شكل هذا الدليل أن يقال : إن كانت الشمس تفسد ، فلابد أن يلحقها ذبول ؛ لكن التالى محال ، فالمقدم محال ، وهذا قياس يسمى عندهم الشرطى المتصل ، وهذه النتيجة غير لازمة ، لأن المقدم غير صحيح ، ما لم يضف إليه شرط آخر ، وهو قوله : إن كانت تفسد فلابد أن تذبل ، فهذا التالى لا يلزم هذا المقدم إلا بزيادة شرط وهو أن نقول : إن كانت تفسد فساداً ذبولياً فلابد أن تذبل في طول المدة ؛ أو يبين أنه لا فساد إلا بطريق الذبول حتى يلزم التالى للمقدم ، ولا نسلم له أنه لا يفسد الشيء إلا بالذبول بل الذبول ، أحد وجوه الفساد ، ولا يبعد أن يفسد الشيء بغتة وهو على حال كماله . الثاني : أنه لو سلم هذا ، وأنه لا فساد إلا بالذبول ، فمن أين عرف أنه لا يعتريها الذبول ؟ ! أما التفاته إلى الأرصاد فمحال ، لأنها لا تعرف مقاديرها إلا بالتقريب ، والشمس التى يقال : إنها كالأرض مائة وسبعين مرة ، أو ما يقرب منه ، لو نقص منها مقدار جبال مثلا ، لكان لا يتبين للحس ، فلعلها في الذبول وإلى الآن قد نقص مقدار جبال فأكثر ، والحس لا يقدر على أن يدرك ذلك ، لأن تقديره في علم المناظر لا يعرف إلا بالتقريب ، وهذا كما أن الياقوت والذهب مركبان من العناصر عندهم وهي قابلة للفساد ، ثم لو وضعت ياقوتة مائة سنة ، لم يكن نقصانها محسوساً ، فلعل نسبة ما ينقص من الشمس في مدة تاريخ الأرصاد كنسبة ما ينقص من الياقوتة في مائة سنة ، وذلك لا يظهر للحس ، فدل أن دليله في غاية الفساد . وقد أعرضنا عن إيراد أدلة كثيرة من هذا الجنس ، يتركها العقلاء ، وأوردنا ههنا هذا الواحد ، ليكون عبرة ومثالا لما تركناه ، واقتصرنا على الأربعة التى تحتاج إلى تكلف في حل شبهتها كما سبق .
The first [argument] is what Galen clung to, when he said: If the sun, for example, were susceptible to annihilation, then over a long period withering would appear in it; and the observations indicating its magnitude for thousands of years indicate only this magnitude. So since it has not withered over these long periods, it indicates that it does not corrupt. The objection to it from several aspects. The first: That the form of this proof is to say: If the sun corrupts, then withering must befall it. But the consequent is impossible, so the antecedent is impossible. This is a syllogism called among them the conditional conjunctive. This conclusion is not necessary, because the antecedent is not valid unless another condition is added to it—namely his statement: 'If it corrupts, it must wither.' So this consequent does not follow from this antecedent except by adding a condition, which is that we say: If it corrupts with a withering corruption, then it must wither over a long period; or that he demonstrate that there is no corruption except by way of withering, so that the consequent follows from the antecedent. And we do not concede to him that a thing is only corrupted by withering; rather withering is one of the modes of corruption, and it is not unlikely that a thing corrupts suddenly while in its state of perfection. The second: That even if this is conceded—that there is no corruption except by withering—how does he know that withering does not affect it? As for his reliance on observations, it is impossible, because they do not know its magnitudes except by approximation. The sun, which is said to be 170 times the earth, or close to that, if an amount like mountains, for example, were to diminish from it, it would not become evident to the senses. Perhaps it is in a state of withering and up to now an amount of mountains or more has diminished, and the senses are unable to perceive that, because its estimation in the science of optics is only known by approximation. This is like ruby and gold, which according to them are composed of elements and are susceptible to corruption. Then if a ruby were placed for a hundred years, its decrease would not be perceptible. So perhaps the proportion of what diminishes from the sun over the period of recorded observations is like the proportion of what diminishes from the ruby in a hundred years, and that does not appear to the senses. Thus it is clear that his proof is extremely corrupt. And we have refrained from presenting many proofs of this kind, which rational people discard, and we have presented here this one, to be a lesson and an example of what we have left aside. And we have confined ourselves to the four [arguments] that require effort in resolving their speciousness, as previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage presents a refutation by Ghazali of an argument attributed to Galen for the incorruptibility of the sun. The passage consists of three parts: first, Galen's original proof; then two distinct objections from Ghazali; and finally a concluding remark about the selection of arguments. Galen's proof is a conditional syllogism: if the sun could be annihilated, it would show signs of withering over time; but astronomical observations over millennia show no change in its size; therefore the sun is incorruptible. Ghazali's first objection attacks the logical form of the conditional. He argues that the consequent (withering) does not necessarily follow from the antecedent (corruption) unless one adds that corruption must be gradual and withering. Without that condition, the syllogism is invalid because corruption could occur suddenly without prior withering. Thus Galen's major premise is not accepted. The second objection concedes, for argument's sake, that all corruption involves withering, but then questions the observational evidence. Ghazali argues that astronomical measurements are only approximate, and the sun's immense size means that a loss of even a mountain-range-sized portion would be imperceptible to the senses. He draws an analogy: a ruby composed of corruptible elements left for a hundred years shows no perceptible decrease, yet it is known to be corruptible. Similarly, the apparent constancy of the sun may hide a gradual but imperceptible diminution. Therefore Galen's proof is unsound. Finally, Ghazali notes that he has omitted many such weak arguments and only includes this one as an example, focusing instead on four arguments that require more detailed refutation. The passage shows Ghazali's skill in logical analysis and his insistence on empirical constraints.

Key Terms
جالينوس (Galen)
الذبول (withering)
الشرطى المتصل (conditional conjunctive syllogism)
الفساد (corruption)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All corruption must be gradual and show visible signs.
Correction: Ghazali argues that corruption can be sudden and without prior withering, so the absence of observed change does not rule out corruption.

Checkpoint
What is the logical form of Galen's argument as identified by Ghazali?
A) Categorical syllogism
B) Conditional conjunctive syllogism (sharṭī muttaṣil)
C) Disjunctive syllogism
D) Inductive reasoning

Key Takeaway
Galen's argument fails because the link between corruption and withering is not necessary; corruption can be sudden.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Where in your life have you assumed that because something has remained constant for a long time, it will never change? What if Allah wills it to vanish suddenly—are you prepared for that loss?
?
Galen observed the sun's constancy for millennia and concluded it cannot perish. What do you rely on today that you treat as if it cannot be taken from you? What would it mean to truly believe that it can vanish 'while in its state of perfection'?
?
If every certainty you hold—your health, your relationships, your faith itself—could be disrupted in an instant, what would you do today to ensure you stand firm when the sudden comes?

Ghazali's Second Objection: The Limits of Observation
Ghazali's empirical objection and concluding remarks

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's second objection to Galen's argument, which challenges the observational evidence and shows how imperceptible changes can occur in large objects.

Core Idea
Observational constancy does not prove incorruptibility when changes may be imperceptible.

Having questioned the logical link, Ghazali now tackles the observational evidence. Even if all corruption involved withering, how do we know the sun hasn't been withering imperceptibly? He uses the analogy of a ruby to show that gradual decrease can be invisible to the senses. Let's explore this second objection.

Guided Path
1
Concede the logical link for argument's sake
Ghazali concedes that if all corruption involves withering, the question becomes: how do we know withering has not occurred?
2
Examine the problem with observations
Ghazali points out that astronomical measurements are only approximate. The sun's huge size means even a loss of 'mountains' would be imperceptible.
He refers to the science of optics (ilm al-manāẓir) to note that estimation is only by approximation.
3
Understand the ruby analogy
A ruby left for a hundred years shows no perceptible decrease, yet it is known to be corruptible (composed of elements). Similarly, the sun may be losing mass imperceptibly.
This analogy reinforces that lack of observed change does not prove incorruptibility.
4
Conclude with Ghazali's summary
Ghazali states that Galen's proof is extremely corrupt, and he only presents this one as an example, having left aside many such weak arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول ما تمسك به جالينوس إذ قال : لو كانت الشمس مثلا تقبل الانعدام ، لظهر فيها ذبول فى مدة مديدة ، والأرصاد الدالة على مقدارها منذ آلاف السنين لا تدل إلا على هذا المقدار ، فلما لم تذبل في هذه الآماد الطويلة ، دل على أنها لا تفسد . الاعتراض عليه من وجوه . الأول : أن شكل هذا الدليل أن يقال : إن كانت الشمس تفسد ، فلابد أن يلحقها ذبول ؛ لكن التالى محال ، فالمقدم محال ، وهذا قياس يسمى عندهم الشرطى المتصل ، وهذه النتيجة غير لازمة ، لأن المقدم غير صحيح ، ما لم يضف إليه شرط آخر ، وهو قوله : إن كانت تفسد فلابد أن تذبل ، فهذا التالى لا يلزم هذا المقدم إلا بزيادة شرط وهو أن نقول : إن كانت تفسد فساداً ذبولياً فلابد أن تذبل في طول المدة ؛ أو يبين أنه لا فساد إلا بطريق الذبول حتى يلزم التالى للمقدم ، ولا نسلم له أنه لا يفسد الشيء إلا بالذبول بل الذبول ، أحد وجوه الفساد ، ولا يبعد أن يفسد الشيء بغتة وهو على حال كماله . الثاني : أنه لو سلم هذا ، وأنه لا فساد إلا بالذبول ، فمن أين عرف أنه لا يعتريها الذبول ؟ ! أما التفاته إلى الأرصاد فمحال ، لأنها لا تعرف مقاديرها إلا بالتقريب ، والشمس التى يقال : إنها كالأرض مائة وسبعين مرة ، أو ما يقرب منه ، لو نقص منها مقدار جبال مثلا ، لكان لا يتبين للحس ، فلعلها في الذبول وإلى الآن قد نقص مقدار جبال فأكثر ، والحس لا يقدر على أن يدرك ذلك ، لأن تقديره في علم المناظر لا يعرف إلا بالتقريب ، وهذا كما أن الياقوت والذهب مركبان من العناصر عندهم وهي قابلة للفساد ، ثم لو وضعت ياقوتة مائة سنة ، لم يكن نقصانها محسوساً ، فلعل نسبة ما ينقص من الشمس في مدة تاريخ الأرصاد كنسبة ما ينقص من الياقوتة في مائة سنة ، وذلك لا يظهر للحس ، فدل أن دليله في غاية الفساد . وقد أعرضنا عن إيراد أدلة كثيرة من هذا الجنس ، يتركها العقلاء ، وأوردنا ههنا هذا الواحد ، ليكون عبرة ومثالا لما تركناه ، واقتصرنا على الأربعة التى تحتاج إلى تكلف في حل شبهتها كما سبق .
The first [argument] is what Galen clung to, when he said: If the sun, for example, were susceptible to annihilation, then over a long period withering would appear in it; and the observations indicating its magnitude for thousands of years indicate only this magnitude. So since it has not withered over these long periods, it indicates that it does not corrupt. The objection to it from several aspects. The first: That the form of this proof is to say: If the sun corrupts, then withering must befall it. But the consequent is impossible, so the antecedent is impossible. This is a syllogism called among them the conditional conjunctive. This conclusion is not necessary, because the antecedent is not valid unless another condition is added to it—namely his statement: 'If it corrupts, it must wither.' So this consequent does not follow from this antecedent except by adding a condition, which is that we say: If it corrupts with a withering corruption, then it must wither over a long period; or that he demonstrate that there is no corruption except by way of withering, so that the consequent follows from the antecedent. And we do not concede to him that a thing is only corrupted by withering; rather withering is one of the modes of corruption, and it is not unlikely that a thing corrupts suddenly while in its state of perfection. The second: That even if this is conceded—that there is no corruption except by withering—how does he know that withering does not affect it? As for his reliance on observations, it is impossible, because they do not know its magnitudes except by approximation. The sun, which is said to be 170 times the earth, or close to that, if an amount like mountains, for example, were to diminish from it, it would not become evident to the senses. Perhaps it is in a state of withering and up to now an amount of mountains or more has diminished, and the senses are unable to perceive that, because its estimation in the science of optics is only known by approximation. This is like ruby and gold, which according to them are composed of elements and are susceptible to corruption. Then if a ruby were placed for a hundred years, its decrease would not be perceptible. So perhaps the proportion of what diminishes from the sun over the period of recorded observations is like the proportion of what diminishes from the ruby in a hundred years, and that does not appear to the senses. Thus it is clear that his proof is extremely corrupt. And we have refrained from presenting many proofs of this kind, which rational people discard, and we have presented here this one, to be a lesson and an example of what we have left aside. And we have confined ourselves to the four [arguments] that require effort in resolving their speciousness, as previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage presents a refutation by Ghazali of an argument attributed to Galen for the incorruptibility of the sun. The passage consists of three parts: first, Galen's original proof; then two distinct objections from Ghazali; and finally a concluding remark about the selection of arguments. Galen's proof is a conditional syllogism: if the sun could be annihilated, it would show signs of withering over time; but astronomical observations over millennia show no change in its size; therefore the sun is incorruptible. Ghazali's first objection attacks the logical form of the conditional. He argues that the consequent (withering) does not necessarily follow from the antecedent (corruption) unless one adds that corruption must be gradual and withering. Without that condition, the syllogism is invalid because corruption could occur suddenly without prior withering. Thus Galen's major premise is not accepted. The second objection concedes, for argument's sake, that all corruption involves withering, but then questions the observational evidence. Ghazali argues that astronomical measurements are only approximate, and the sun's immense size means that a loss of even a mountain-range-sized portion would be imperceptible to the senses. He draws an analogy: a ruby composed of corruptible elements left for a hundred years shows no perceptible decrease, yet it is known to be corruptible. Similarly, the apparent constancy of the sun may hide a gradual but imperceptible diminution. Therefore Galen's proof is unsound. Finally, Ghazali notes that he has omitted many such weak arguments and only includes this one as an example, focusing instead on four arguments that require more detailed refutation. The passage shows Ghazali's skill in logical analysis and his insistence on empirical constraints.

Key Terms
الأرصاد (observations)
التقريب (approximation)
المناظر (optics)
الفساد (corruption)
الياقوت (ruby)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If an object shows no observable change over a long period, it must be incorruptible.
Correction: Ghazali shows that imperceptible changes can occur, so lack of observed change does not prove incorruptibility.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Ghazali use to illustrate that a lack of perceptible change does not prove incorruptibility?
A) A mountain eroding over time
B) A ruby left for a hundred years showing no perceptible decrease
C) Water evaporating from a lake
D) Gold melting in a furnace

Key Takeaway
Observational constancy does not rule out imperceptible decay; thus Galen's empirical argument is unsound.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You trust your senses and instruments to tell you the truth about the world. But Ghazali shows that even careful observation can miss subtle decay. What in your own life have you assumed to be unchanging—your faith, your character, your relationships—that might actually be withering imperceptibly? How will you check for that decay before it becomes undeniable?
?
Ghazali uses the example of a ruby to show that things can corrupt slowly over time without being noticed. What spiritual 'ruby' have you placed on a shelf for years, assuming it's safe, without inspecting it for wear? What would it take to examine it today?
?
The philosophers relied on observational constancy to prove the sun's incorruptibility, but they were wrong. Where in your own understanding of Islam and the world might you be overconfident in what seems constant? Are you willing to admit that your current state might be corrupting without your noticing? What step will you take this week to uncover hidden decay?

Ghazali's Methodological Selectivity in Refutation
Ghazali explains his selective methodology in refuting philosophers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's strategic approach in Tahafut al-Falasifa: why he omits many arguments and focuses only on the four that require careful effort to resolve.

Core Idea
Ghazali only refutes arguments worth refuting.

In this passage, Ghazali explains his methodological choice to omit weak arguments and concentrate on the most challenging ones. This reveals his strategic approach to philosophical refutation: quality over quantity. By focusing only on the four proofs that require 'takalluf' (effort) to resolve their fallacy, Ghazali demonstrates that he is not interested in easy targets but in the strongest objections to Islamic beliefs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Pay attention to the key terms: عبرة, تكلف, شبهة.
2
Identify what Ghazali omitted and why
Ghazali says he refrained from presenting many proofs 'which the rational leave.' Why would rational people discard these arguments?
He includes one example only as a lesson (عبرة) for what was omitted.
3
Understand the focus on four proofs
He limited himself to the four that require 'takalluf' (effort) in resolving their 'shubha' (ambiguity). Reflect on what this implies about his selection criteria.
These four are the ones he previously mentioned and will refute in detail.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد أعرضنا عن إيراد أدلة كثيرة من هذا الجنس، يتركها العقلاء، وأوردنا ههنا هذا الواحد، ليكون عبرة ومثالا لما تركناه، واقتصرنا على الأربعة التى تحتاج إلى تكلف في حل شبهتها كما سبق.
We have refrained from presenting many proofs of this kind, which the rational leave, and we have presented this one here to serve as a lesson and example for what we have omitted. We limited ourselves to the four [proofs] that require effort in resolving their ambiguity, as mentioned.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage is a methodological note by Ghazali explaining his selective approach in refuting the philosophers. He states that he has omitted many arguments that reasonable people would discard anyway. He presents only one example from that category to serve as a lesson. Then he says he restricts himself to the four proofs that require careful effort to resolve their fallacies, as previously mentioned. This shows Ghazali's strategic focus on the strongest philosophical arguments rather than wasting time on weak ones.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's selective method in Tahafut al-Falasifa demonstrates his strategic focus on refuting the most challenging philosophical arguments rather than wasting effort on weak ones.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What is one ambiguous question you have been avoiding, and how will you apply takalluf to resolve it this week?

Refuting Philosophers on the First Effect
Ghazali refutes philosophers' first effect claim and advises limited inquiry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali rejects the philosophers' explanation of the first effect and why he directs believers to accept prophetic teachings without delving into the modality, quantity, or quiddity of divine matters.

Core Idea
Reason has limits: accept prophetic principles, don't dissect God's essence.

Imam al-Ghazali critiques the philosophers' attempt to explain the emanation of the universe from the First Principle. He finds their reasoning incoherent—a 'stupidity' with no plausible connection. Then he offers a methodological principle: accept the fundamentals from the prophets and do not pry into the modality, quantity, or quiddity of divine matters, for human reason is not equipped for that. This lesson unpacks that critique and the resulting guidance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the shift from refutation to directive.
2
Identify the refutation
Find where Ghazali critiques the philosophers' claim. What does he call it? Why does he reject it?
He calls it 'حماقة' (stupidity) because it lacks suitability.
3
Understand the methodological directive
Pay attention to his call to accept from prophets and limit inquiry. What is the reason? What hadith does he cite?
Reason does not deem it impossible, so we accept. Inquiry into divine essence is beyond human capacity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلى أن المعلول الأول ، من حيث إنه ممكن الوجود ، صدر منه فلك ، ومن حيث إنه يعقل نفسه ، صدر منه نفس الفلك ، وهذه حماقة لا إظهار مناسبة فلنتقبل مبادئ هذه الأمور من الأنبياء - صلوات الله عليهم - وليصدقوا فيها ؛ إذ العقل ليس يحيلها ، وليترك البحث عن الكيفية والكمية والماهية ، فليس ذلك مما تتسع له القوى البشرية ، ولذلك قال صاحب الشرع - صلوات الله عليه - : ( تفكروا فى خلق الله ، ولا تفكروا في ذات الله )
That the first effect, inasmuch as it is possible of existence, issued from it a sphere, and inasmuch as it intellects itself, issued from it the soul of the sphere. And this is a stupidity showing no suitability. So let us accept the principles of these matters from the prophets—peace be upon them—and believe in them, since reason does not deem them impossible, and let us leave off inquiry into the modality, quantity, and quiddity, for that is not something that human powers encompass. Therefore the possessor of the Law—peace be upon him—said: 'Reflect upon the creation of God, and do not reflect upon the essence of God.'
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage is a refutation and a methodological directive. Ghazali first exposes the absurdity of the philosophers' claim that the first effect (the first intellect) emanates a sphere from one aspect and a soul from another. He calls this a stupidity (حماقة) because it lacks a plausible connection. Then he advises that the principles of such matters should be taken from the prophets and believed in, as reason does not deem them impossible. He then warns against inquiring into the how, how much, and whatness (modality, quantity, quiddity) of divine matters, as human powers are insufficient. He supports this with the hadith: 'Reflect upon the creation of God, and do not reflect upon the essence of God.' Thus, the passage serves to limit the scope of rational inquiry and affirm the need for prophetic guidance.

العقل
الكيفية
الكمية
الماهية
القوى البشرية
صاحب الشرع
خلق الله
ذات الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ghazali rejects all rational inquiry outright.
Correction: He limits inquiry to creation, not divine essence. Reason is used to accept what is not impossible, but metaphysical details are left to revelation.

Checkpoint

B) It is a stupidity showing no suitability
C) It is a sound argument
D) It is acceptable if interpreted metaphorically

Key Takeaway
Ghazali critiques the philosophers' incoherent emanation theory and teaches that human reason should accept prophetic principles without attempting to dissect divine essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a matter of faith that your reason cannot fully grasp, do you trust the prophetic guidance or demand full understanding before submitting? Recall a specific instance.
?
The hadith commands reflection on creation, not on God's essence. Where does your own contemplation usually focus—on the intricacies of creation or on speculating about the nature of Allah? What will you change starting today?
?
Ghazali calls the philosophers' attempt to explain emanation a 'stupidity.' Are there areas in your own religious understanding where you have over-rationalized beyond what is given? How can you realign with the prophetic method this week?
