Ghazali's Method of Refutation
Ghazali justifies his practice of fully presenting opponents' doctrines before refuting them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali believed it was necessary to fully present the arguments of his opponents before refuting them, and how he responded to those who criticized this method.

Core Idea
To refute a widespread error, you must first present it accurately.

In this autobiographical passage, Ghazali defends his controversial method of elaborating his opponents' arguments. Critics said he was helping the heretics. Drawing on the precedent of Ahmad ibn Hanbal, Ghazali explains when and how it is permissible—even necessary—to narrate a doubt before answering it.

Guided Path
1
Read Ghazali's initial approach
Notice how Ghazali describes collecting and arranging the Batinites' doctrines alongside the truth before refuting them.
2
Identify the criticism
The 'people of truth' accused Ghazali of supporting the heretics by elaborating their arguments. The analogy with Ahmad ibn Hanbal and al-Muhasibi illustrates the concern.
3
Understand Ghazali's response
Ghazali distinguishes between obscure and famous doubts, stating that only widespread doubts require full exposition before refutation. He also insists he did not fabricate the doubt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فابتدأت بطلب كتبهم، وجمع مقالاتهم، وكان قد بلغنى بعض كلماتهم المستحدثة التي ولدتها خواطر أهل العصر، لا على المنهاج المعهود من سلفهم، فجمعت تلك الكلمات، ورتبتها ترتيباً محكماً، مقارناً للحق، واستوفيت الجواب عنها، حتى أنكر أهل الحق منى، مبالغتي في تقرير حجتهم، وقال: هذا سعى لهم فإنهم كانوا يعجزون عن نصرة مذهبهم، لولا تحقيقك له، وترتيبك إياه، وهذا الإنكار من وجه حق، فلقد أنكر أحمد بن حنبل، على الحارث المحاسبي – رحمهما الله - تصنيفه في الرد على المعتزلة، فقال الحارث: الرد على البدعة فرض؛ فقال أحمد: نعم، ولكن حکیت شبهتهم أولا، ثم أجبت عنها، فبم تأمن أن يطالع الشبهة من يعلق ذلك بفهمه، ولا يلتفت إلى الجواب، أو ينظر إلى الجواب، ولا يفهم كنهه؟ ! وما ذكره أحمد حق، ولكن فى شبهة لم تنتشر، ولم تشتهر، فأما إذا انتشرت فالجواب عنها واجب، ولا يمكن الجواب عنها، إلا بعد الحكاية؛ نعم ينبغى ألا يتكلف لهم شبهة، ولم أتكلف أنا ذلك، بل كنت قد سمعت تلك الشبهة من واحد من أصحابى المختلفين إلى، بعد أن كان قد التحق بهم، وانتحل مذهبهم، وحكى أنهم يضحكون على تصانيف المصنفين في الرد عليهم، فإنهم لم يفهموا بعد حجتهم، وذكر تلك الحجة وحكاها عنهم؛ فلم أرض لنفسى أن يظن بي الغفلة عن أصل حجتهم، فلذلك أوردتها؛ ولا أن يظن بي أنى - وإن سمعتها - فلم أفهمها، فلذلك قررتها، والمقصود أني قررت شبهتهم إلى أقصى الإمكان، ثم أظهرت فسادها بغاية البرهان.
So I began by seeking their books and collecting their doctrines. Some of their newly invented sayings, generated by the thoughts of the people of the age, not on the accustomed path of their predecessors, had reached me. So I collected those sayings and arranged them in a precise order, comparing them with the truth, and I fully responded to them, until the people of truth criticized me for my excessive elaboration in presenting their argument, saying: 'This is support for them, for they were unable to defend their doctrine until you verified it and arranged it.' This criticism is in a sense valid, for Ahmad ibn Hanbal criticized Al-Harith al-Muhasibi—may God have mercy on them both—for his composition refuting the Mu'tazila. Al-Harith said: 'Refuting innovation is an obligation.' Ahmad replied: 'Yes, but you first narrated their doubt, then answered it. How can you be sure that someone who reads the doubt will have it stick in his mind, and not pay attention to the answer, or look at the answer without understanding its essence?' And what Ahmad mentioned is true, but [it applies] to a doubt that has not spread and has not become famous. As for when it has spread, answering it is obligatory, and it is not possible to answer it except after presenting it. Indeed, one should not fabricate a doubt for them, and I did not do that. Rather, I had heard that doubt from one of my companions who used to visit me, after he had joined them and adopted their doctrine. He told me that they laugh at the compositions of those who refute them because they have not yet understood their argument. He mentioned that argument and narrated it from them. So I was not content to have it thought of me that I was heedless of the basis of their argument—therefore I presented it; nor that it be thought that though I had heard it, I did not understand it—therefore I expounded it. My purpose was that I presented their doubt to the fullest extent possible, then demonstrated its corruption with the utmost proof.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is a first-person account by Ghazali explaining why he composed a book presenting the doctrine of the Batinites before refuting them. He begins by describing his method: he collected their books and sayings, arranged them carefully alongside the truth, and provided full refutations. Some critics objected that by elaborating the opponents' argument he was actually helping them, citing the precedent of Ahmad ibn Hanbal's criticism of al-Muhasibi's refutation of the Mu'tazila. Al-Muhasibi argued that refuting innovation is obligatory, but Ahmad cautioned that narrating the doubt first might lead some readers to be influenced by it without understanding the answer. Ghazali counters that this caution applies only to obscure doubts; if a doubt has spread, refutation is obligatory and requires prior exposition. He insists he did not fabricate the doubt but heard it from a former follower who reported that the Batinites mocked previous refutations as ignorant of their real arguments. Ghazali therefore presented the doubt fully to prove his understanding and then refuted it with complete proof.

Key Terms
شبهة
رد
بدعة
حكاية المذهب
تقرير الحجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Presenting an opponent's argument fully is always harmful, or that Ghazali invented doubts for the Batinites.
Correction: Ghazali argues that his method is necessary when the doubt has spread, and he did not fabricate it but heard it from a former follower.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, under what condition is it permissible to narrate an opponent's doubt before answering it?
A) It is never permissible.
B) When the doubt has spread and become famous.
C) When the opponent is weak.
D) When the refuter is a scholar.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali distinguishes between obscure and widespread doubts: the latter require full presentation for effective refutation, provided the doubt is not fabricated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a doubt about your faith, do you immediately try to refute it or do you first seek to understand it fully? Which approach would Ghazali say is necessary if that doubt has become widespread among people you know? Will you commit to understanding the strongest form of a doubt before attempting to answer it?
?
Ghazali was criticized by the people of truth for his elaboration, yet he persisted because he knew the doubt had spread. Have you ever been misunderstood or criticized when you tried to engage with a false belief? How does Ghazali's reasoning encourage you to persist in seeking truth even when others question your methods?
?
Ahmad ibn Hanbal warned that narrating a doubt could lead some astray. Think of someone you care about who has been influenced by a doubt about Islam. What responsibility does this lesson place on you to both understand that doubt and present the answer wisely? Will you take that step today?

Ghazali's Critique of Earlier Theologians
Ghazali criticizes previous theologians' refutations for inadequate understanding.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali found earlier theologians' refutations of the philosophers to be inadequate and how this motivated his own approach.

Core Idea
Earlier refutations failed because they misunderstood the philosophers' actual arguments.

In this passage, Ghazali explains why he was dissatisfied with previous attempts to refute the philosophers. He observed that earlier theologians did not accurately represent philosophical doctrines, mixing them into confusing and contradictory claims. This realization prompted Ghazali to adopt a new method: first faithfully presenting the philosophers' views, then refuting them systematically.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Let Ghazali's own words set the tone.
Notice the strong language used: 'intricate, scattered, manifestly contradictory and corrupt.'
2
Identify Ghazali's specific criticisms
Identify what Ghazali says about the theologians' refutations. Focus on the three adjectives he uses to describe their quotations: 'intricate', 'scattered', and 'contradictory and corrupt'.
3
Reflect on the motivational context
Consider why Ghazali concludes with 'So I knew.' This indicates his realization that a better approach is needed, which he undertakes in the Tahafut.
This step connects the critique to Ghazali's own methodology.

Source Text

ولم يكن في كتب المتكلمين، من كلامهم – يعنى الفلاسفة - حيث اشتغلوا بالرد عليهم، إلا كلمات معقدة مبددة، ظاهرة التناقض والفساد، لا يظن الاغترار بها بغافل عامى، فضلا عمن يدعى دقائق العلوم، فعلمت.
And there was nothing in the books of the theologians from their (i.e., the philosophers') discourse, when they occupied themselves with refuting them, except intricate, scattered words, manifestly contradictory and corrupt, such that even an ignorant common person would not be deluded by them, let alone one who claims subtle knowledge. Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This brief passage is Ghazali's direct critique of previous theologians' attempts to refute the philosophers. He states that the theologians' books contained only 'intricate, scattered words' from the philosophers—meaning they quoted philosophical statements in a confused, disjointed manner. These quotations were so clearly contradictory and corrupt that even a layman would not be fooled, much less a scholar. Ghazali implies that this poor understanding made their refutations ineffective. He concludes 'so I knew', indicating that this observation motivated him to adopt his own method of first thoroughly presenting the philosophers' doctrines before refuting them, as exemplified in the Tahafut.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction

Correction: Ghazali considered them poorly executed, not entirely worthless; his critique focuses on the method of quoting and refuting, implying that a more accurate approach could be effective.

Key Takeaway

Ghazali's Methodological Principle for Refuting Heresies
Ghazali explains his careful method of refutation using the Batiniyya example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the principle of faithfully presenting an opponent's argument before refuting it, as exemplified by Imam Ghazali's approach to the Batiniyya.

Core Idea
Faithful refutation requires first accurately understanding the opposing view.

Imam Ghazali was criticized for presenting the arguments of the Batiniyya too thoroughly. In this passage, he defends his method by citing the precedent of Ahmad ibn Hanbal and explaining the conditions under which narrating a doubt is not only permissible but obligatory. This lesson explores the balance between engaging with error and avoiding its spread.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noting Ghazali's narrative of his method and the objection he faced.
2
Identify the objection
Find the criticism from 'the people of truth' and Ahmad ibn Hanbal's point about the danger of narrating doubts.
3
Analyze Ghazali's defense
Notice how Ghazali distinguishes between obscure and widespread doubts, and his personal reason for presenting the argument fully.
4
Connect to the broader principle
Consider how this principle applies to refuting any false doctrine, including the philosophers.
This lesson establishes a key rule for ilm al-kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فابتدأت بطلب كتبهم، وجمع مقالاتهم، وكان قد بلغني بعض كلماتهم المستحدثة التي ولدتها خواطر أهل العصر، لا على المنهاج المعهود من سلفهم، فجمعت تلك الكلمات، ورتبتها ترتيباً محكماً، مقارناً للحق، واستوفيت الجواب عنها، حتى أنكر أهل الحق مني، مبالغتي في تقرير حجتهم، وقال: هذا سعى لهم فإنهم كانوا يعجزون عن نصرة مذهبهم، لولا تحقيقك له، وترتيبك إياه، وهذا الإنكار من وجه حق، فلقد أنكر أحمد بن حنبل، على الحارث المحاسبي – رحمهما الله - تصنيفه في الرد على المعتزلة، فقال الحارث: الرد على البدعة فرض؛ فقال أحمد: نعم، ولكن حكيت شبهتهم أولا، ثم أجبت عنها، فبم تأمن أن يطالع الشبهة من يعلق ذلك بفهمه، ولا يلتفت إلى الجواب، أو ينظر إلى الجواب، ولا يفهم كنهه؟! وما ذكره أحمد حق، ولكن في شبهة لم تنتشر، ولم تشتهر، فأما إذا انتشرت فالجواب عنها واجب، ولا يمكن الجواب عنها إلا بعد الحكاية؛ نعم ينبغي ألا يتكلف لهم شبهة، ولم أتكلف أنا ذلك، بل كنت قد سمعت تلك الشبهة من واحد من أصحابي المختلفين إلي، بعد أن كان قد التحق بهم، وانتحل مذهبهم، وحكى أنهم يضحكون على تصانيف المصنفين في الرد عليهم، فإنهم لم يفهموا بعد حجتهم، وذكر تلك الحجة وحكاها عنهم؛ فلم أرض لنفسي أن يظن بي الغفلة عن أصل حجتهم، فلذلك أوردتها؛ ولا أن يظن بي أني - وإن سمعتها - فلم أفهمها، فلذلك قررتها، والمقصود أني قررت شبهتهم إلى أقصى الإمكان، ثم أظهرت فسادها بغاية البرهان
I began by seeking their books and collecting their sayings, and some of their novel words had reached me, which the thoughts of the people of the age had generated, not upon the accustomed path of their predecessors. So I collected those words and arranged them in a solid arrangement, comparing them to the truth, and I completely fulfilled the answer to them, until the people of truth disapproved of my exaggeration in presenting their argument, and they said: 'This is advocacy for them, for they would be unable to defend their school were it not for your verification and ordering of it.' And this disapproval is in one respect correct, for Ahmad ibn Hanbal – may Allah have mercy on both of them – disapproved of al-Harith al-Muhasibi's composition in refuting the Mu'tazila. Al-Harith said: 'Refuting innovation is an obligation.' Ahmad said: 'Yes, but you first narrated their doubt, then answered it. What assurance do you have that one who studies the doubt might become attached to it in his understanding and not pay attention to the answer, or look at the answer but not understand its essence?' What Ahmad mentioned is true, but regarding a doubt that had not spread and become famous. As for when it has spread, answering it is obligatory, and answering it is not possible except after narrating it. Yes, one should not go to the trouble of inventing a doubt for them, and I did not go to that trouble. Rather, I had heard that doubt from one of my associates who had joined them and adopted their school, and he related that they laugh at the compositions of writers who refute them, for they have not yet understood their argument. And he mentioned that argument and narrated it from them. So I was not content for myself that it be thought of me that I was heedless of their original argument – therefore I presented it; nor that it be thought that I – even if I heard it – did not understand it – therefore I stated it firmly. And the point is that I presented their doubt to the utmost possible, then I showed its corruption with the utmost proof.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is a narrative explanation by Imam Ghazali of his methodology for refuting the Batiniyya sect, using it as a model for all refutations, including that of the philosophers. Ghazali begins by describing his preparatory steps: he sought out their books, collected their doctrines, and arranged them systematically, comparing them to the truth. He then composed comprehensive responses. Some critics accused him of overstating their case, effectively advocating for them. Ghazali acknowledges the validity of this concern by citing the precedent of Ahmad ibn Hanbal's objection to al-Harith al-Muhasibi: Ahmad argued that narrating a doubt before answering it risks the reader becoming ensnared by the doubt without properly grasping the refutation. Ghazali argues, however, that this risk applies only to obscure innovations that have not yet spread. Once an erroneous doctrine has become widespread, it is obligatory to refute it, and such refutation necessarily requires a clear exposition of the argument being refuted. Ghazali maintains that he did not fabricate any argument; rather, he heard the Batiniyya's arguments from a former associate who had adopted their views. The associate reported that the Batiniyya laugh at existing refutations because the refuters did not accurately understand their arguments. Ghazali therefore resolved to present their argument accurately and completely, and then demolish it with conclusive proof. This establishes the principle that a refutation must be preceded by a faithful understanding of the position being refuted.

Key Terms
الرد على البدعة
الشبهة
تقرير الحجة
الحكاية قبل الجواب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that presenting an opponent's argument in detail constitutes endorsing it or spreading it unnecessarily.
Correction: Ghazali shows that when a false doctrine has become widespread, it is obligatory to refute it, and faithful narration is essential for an effective refutation. He did not invent arguments but reported what was already being said.

Checkpoint
Why did Ghazali choose to present the Batiniyya's arguments in full detail before refuting them?
A) Because he wanted to show off his knowledge of their doctrines.
B) Because he feared being accused of ignorance or misunderstanding of their arguments.
C) Because he secretly agreed with them and wanted to spread their ideas.
D) Because he was ordered by the caliph to do so.

Key Takeaway
A proper refutation requires accurate and complete narration of the opposing argument, especially when that argument has become widespread.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you honestly try to understand the arguments of those you disagree with, or do you dismiss them without hearing them out? Ghazali deliberately listened to his associate who joined the Batiniyya. When someone opposes your beliefs, do you rush to refute or first seek to truly understand? What will you do differently this week to ensure you defend truth with knowledge, not assumption?
?
Ahmad ibn Hanbal warned that narrating a doubt can cause someone to become attached to it without understanding the answer. Have you ever shared a doubt or a criticism of Islam without being able to refute it? Whom might you have harmed by doing so? How will you guard your tongue and your writings from spreading confusion?
?
Ghazali was confident that truth would prevail, so he did not fear studying falsehood. Are you afraid that reading the arguments of atheists or heretics might shake your faith? If so, what does that say about your certainty? Will you commit to strengthening your faith until it can withstand any challenge, just as Ghazali did?

Ghazali's Critique of Inadequate Refutations
Ghazali criticizes earlier refutations and states a methodological principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's criticism of earlier theologians' refutation methods and the principle that one must understand a doctrine before refuting it.

Core Idea
Refuting a doctrine without understanding it is like shooting in the dark.

Ghazali opens his Tahafut by addressing a critical methodological flaw in the works of earlier theologians. He argues that they attempted to refute the philosophers without properly grasping their views, resulting in weak and self-contradictory arguments. This lesson examines his sharp critique and the principle he establishes, which shapes his own approach in the book.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice Ghazali's tone and the key descriptors he uses for the earlier refutations.
Pay attention to the words 'معقدة مبددة' and 'رمي في عماية'.
2
Identify Ghazali's criticism
What specific words does he use to characterize the theologians' representation? Why does he say even a layperson would not be deceived?
He uses 'ظاهرة التناقض والفساد' — apparent contradiction and corruption.
3
Grasp the epistemological principle
Extract the lesson Ghazali draws – refuting before understanding is like shooting in the dark. Why is this important for his project?
This principle motivates Ghazali to first faithfully present the philosophers' views in Maqasid al-Falasifa.
4
Consider the broader context
How does this critique set the stage for Ghazali's own method in Tahafut?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم يكن في كتب المتكلمين، من كلامهم – يعني الفلاسفة – حيث اشتغلوا بالرد عليهم، إلا كلمات معقدة مبددة، ظاهرة التناقض والفساد، لا يظن الاغترار بها بغافل عامي، فضلا عمن يدعي دقائق العلوم، فعلمت أن رد المذهب قبل فهمه والاطلاع على كنهه رمي في عماية
And there was nothing in the books of the theologians (mutakallimun) of their speech – that is, the philosophers – when they engaged in refuting them, except complex, scattered words, apparent in self-contradiction and corruption, such that not even an ignorant commoner would be deceived by them, let alone one who claims subtle knowledge. So I knew that refuting a school before understanding it and becoming acquainted with its essence is shooting in the dark.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is Ghazali's harsh assessment of the efforts of earlier theologians (mutakallimun) who attempted to refute the philosophers. He states that the representation of the philosophers' views found in those refutations was nothing but 'complex, scattered words' – disjointed and unclear – which were obviously contradictory and corrupt. This is a strong indictment: even an ignorant layperson would not be fooled by such weak presentations, much less someone claiming knowledge of subtle sciences. Ghazali then draws the moral: to refute a doctrine without first understanding it and grasping its essence is like shooting blindly ('shooting in the dark'). This serves as the justification for his own methodology – he will first faithfully present the philosophers' arguments (in his earlier work Maqasid al-Falasifa) before refuting them (in Tahafut al-Falasifa).

Key Terms
المتكلمين
كلمات معقدة مبددة
رمي في عماية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ghazali rejected all theology (kalam).
Correction: Ghazali only criticizes the execution of refutations, not the discipline of kalam itself.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what characterized the refutations of the philosophers by earlier theologians?
A) They were clear and well-argued.
B) They were complex, scattered, and obviously contradictory.
C) They were based on scripture rather than reason.
D) They were mostly respectful and accurate.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali insists that effective refutation requires first faithfully understanding the opposing view.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you dismissed or argued against a belief (religious, political, or personal) without truly understanding it. In light of Ghazali's principle, does that criticism hold any weight? Will you commit to seeking understanding before rejecting in the future?
?
When you encounter a person whose views strongly oppose yours, do you feel a rush to refute them? How might your response change if you first aimed to grasp their perspective 'in its essence' (bi-kawnihi)? What one step will you take this week to practice this?
?
Ghazali says even an ignorant commoner would not be deceived by a distorted refutation. Are there areas in your faith where you rely on weak or oversimplified arguments from others? Will you stop yourself from using such arguments and instead invest in deeper learning?

Ghazali's Methodical Transition to the Five Ranks
Ghazali transitions to a structured refutation of five ranks.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the purpose of Ghazali's transition statement and how he structures his upcoming refutation around five separate questions targeting each rank of multiplicity.

Core Idea
Ghazali structures his refutation around five separate questions, each targeting a rank of multiplicity.

This brief but crucial sentence marks a turning point in Ghazali's argument. He is not digressing; he is methodically planning to dismantle the philosophers' position on multiplicity. He announces a return to a previously introduced framework — the five ranks of multiplicity — and declares his intention to treat each rank in a separate, self-contained question. This sets up a clear, organized refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic sentence once, then the literal translation. Notice the key verbs: 'return', 'show', 'draw up'.
This is a short statement, so read carefully for its structural cues.
2
Identify the three announced actions
Ghazali says: 1) We return to the five ranks, 2) We show their inability to prove their denial, 3) We draw up a separate question for each. Each action is a step in his method.
3
Understand the 'five ranks'
The five ranks are aspects of multiplicity (kathra) that the philosophers deny in the divine essence. They were likely introduced earlier. This lesson does not detail them, but you should know they are the target.
The following lessons will treat each rank individually.
4
Recognize the methodological shift
Ghazali moves from general critique to specific refutations. He will dedicate a separate mas'ala (question) to each rank, ensuring thorough treatment.
This shows Ghazali's systematic approach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنعد الآن إلى المراتب الخمسة في أقسام الكثرة، ودعواهم نفيها، ونبين عجزهم عن إقامة الدليل، ولنرسم لكل واحدة مسألة على حيالها
And let us now return to the five ranks in the divisions of multiplicity, and their claim of denying it, and we will show their inability to establish proof, and we will draw up for each one a separate question.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is a short transitional statement in which Ghazali signals a return to the core topic of the Tahafut: the philosophers' denial of multiplicity (kathra) in the divine essence. He refers to 'five ranks' (al-maratib al-khamsa) in the divisions of multiplicity – these are the aspects of multiplicity that the philosophers reject in their theory of emanation. Ghazali announces that he will demonstrate the philosophers' inability to prove their position, and he will treat each rank in a separate question (mas'ala). This sets up a methodical, structured refutation, each question focusing on one aspect of the philosophers' doctrine.

Key Terms
المراتب الخمسة
أقسام الكثرة
إقامة الدليل
مسألة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ghazali is abandoning his methodological discussion or summarily dismissing the philosophers.
Correction: Rather, he is applying his principle of understanding before refuting by systematically addressing each rank of multiplicity in a separate, argued question.

Checkpoint

A) Prove that they exist in the divine essence.
B) Show that the philosophers cannot prove their denial of these ranks.
C) Accept the philosophers' view on each rank.
D) Argue that there are more than five ranks.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali announces a methodical refutation of the philosophers' denial of multiplicity through five separate questions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ghazali does not rush to refute but first announces a structured plan. When you encounter a view you disagree with, do you first organize your objections into distinct questions, or do you react emotionally? How can you imitate this discipline starting today?
?
The philosophers' denial of multiplicity is broken into five separate ranks. What is one area of your belief where you have assumed a single simple answer, but might actually need to examine multiple distinct aspects? Will you take time this week to list those aspects?

Understanding Before Refuting: A Kalam Methodological Principle
Commentator defends Ghazali's purpose in writing Maqasid al-Falasifa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is necessary to fully comprehend a doctrine before attempting to refute it, using Ghazali's approach as a model.

Core Idea
Rejecting a doctrine without understanding it is shooting in the dark.

The commentator here addresses a common methodological flaw: attacking a position without first grasping its full meaning. He uses Ghazali's own practice as an example: Ghazali wrote 'Maqasid al-Falasifa' not merely as a summary to be refuted, but as a sincere exposition to demonstrate his complete understanding. This lesson unpacks that principle and its implications for scholarly debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the commentator's argument.
Notice the use of rhetorical questions in the latter part.
2
Identify the core principle
Find the phrase that states the main methodological rule: 'رد المذهب قبل فهمه رمى في عماية'. This is the central warning.
3
Analyze Ghazali's example
The commentator applies the principle to Ghazali. Reflect on why Ghazali wrote 'Maqasid al-Falasifa' and how it relates to 'Tahafut al-Falasifa'. What was the commentator's conclusion about Ghazali's purpose?
Consider the rhetorical questions: why include logic and natural sciences if no disagreement?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنعد الآن إلى المراتب الخمسة فى أقسام الكثرة ، ودعواهم نفيها ، ونبين عجرهم عن إقامة الدليل ، ولنرسم لكل واحدة مسألة على حيالها .. أن رد المذهب قبل فهمه والاطلاع على كنهه رمى في عماية ] وهو لذلك يعلم أن الفلاسفة كانوا يعيبون فيهم هذا النقص ، فأراد أن يتجمل بالبراءة منه ، بتأليف هذا الكتاب » مقاصد الفلاسفة » ، وإلا فما باله يطيل فى كتاب « تهافت الفلاسفة » شرحاً وإيضاحاً بما يجعلنا في غنى مطلق عن ذلك الكتاب ؟ ! وما باله أيضاً يتعرض في ذلك الكتاب لما لم يكن موضع خلاف بينه وبين الفلاسفة ، فقد تعرض فيه الجميع مسائل المنطق وليس يخالفهم في بذلك في كتاب ( التهافت » وتعرض لكل مسائلهم الطبيعية ، وليس يخالفهم إلا في مسائل معدودة منها ؟ ! في حين أنه قد أسعفنا في « التهافت » بشرح ما يريد الرد عليه من شيء منها كما صرح ، وعرضه عرضاً واضحاً ، قبل الرد عليه ، بحيث لا يشعر القارئ ، بأنه في حاجة إلى شيء آخر أصلا ، فلا بد أن يكون لتأليف ذلك الكتاب غرض آخر وراء التمهيد لكتاب تهافت الفلاسفة ، وهو ما أوضحناه .
And let us now return to the five ranks in the divisions of multiplicity, and their claim of negating it, and we shall show their inability to establish proof, and we shall outline for each one a separate issue. That rejecting a doctrine before understanding it and comprehending its essence is shooting in the dark. And he therefore knows that the philosophers used to criticize them for this deficiency, so he wanted to adorn himself with innocence from it by composing this book 'Maqasid al-Falasifa'. Otherwise, why would he elaborate in the book 'Tahafut al-Falasifa' with explanation and clarification that makes us completely independent of that book? And why would he also discuss in that book what was not a point of disagreement between him and the philosophers? For he discussed all the issues of logic in it, yet he does not disagree with them in that in the book 'Tahafut', and he discussed all their natural issues, yet he does not disagree with them except in a few of them?! While he has provided us in 'Tahafut' with an explanation of what he intends to refute of their views, as he stated, and presented it clearly before refuting it, such that the reader does not feel that he needs anything else at all. So there must be another purpose for composing that book beyond preparing for the book 'Tahafut al-Falasifa', which is what we have explained.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is an editorial commentary defending Ghazali against the charge that he wrote 'Maqasid al-Falasifa' merely as a preface to 'Tahafut al-Falasifa'. The commentator argues that some people reject a doctrine without understanding it, which is like shooting in the dark. Ghazali, aware that the philosophers criticized Muslims for this, wrote 'Maqasid al-Falasifa' to show he understood their views thoroughly. The commentator then raises rhetorical questions: If 'Tahafut' alone suffices with its clear exposition and refutation, why write 'Maqasid'? And why include in 'Maqasid' subjects like logic and most natural philosophy where there is no disagreement? The answer is that 'Maqasid' must have had a purpose beyond merely introducing 'Tahafut'—namely, to demonstrate Ghazali's complete grasp of the philosophers' system before refuting it.

Key Terms
رد المذهب
فهم
مقاصد الفلاسفة
تهافت الفلاسفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ghazali's Maqasid al-Falasifa was only a summary to be refuted in Tahafut.
Correction: In fact, it was to prove he understood philosophy before criticizing it, and the commentator argues there was additional purpose.

Checkpoint
What does the commentator say about rejecting a doctrine before understanding it?
A) It is the proper scholarly method.
B) It is shooting in the dark.
C) It is recommended to save time.
D) It is what the philosophers always did.

Key Takeaway
A scholar must fully understand a doctrine before attempting to refute it, lest he be guilty of ignorance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief you hold strongly that you have never seriously examined from the opposing side. What is one specific step you can take this week to truly understand that view, even if you continue to disagree?
?
When you find yourself dismissing an argument or a person's position, do you first pause to ensure you can articulate their position as they would? If not, what prevents you from doing so?
?
Ghazali invested a whole book to demonstrate his understanding before he refuted. What does this say about the depth of intellectual integrity required? In what area of your own life—studies, work, or personal relationships—are you most guilty of 'shooting in the dark'? What will you change starting today?

Ghazali's Method: Refutation Without Construction
Ghazali responds that his book aims to refute, not to ground truth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's methodological stance in Tahafut al-Falasifa and why he is not required to defend a positive position.

Core Idea
Tahafut al-Falasifa is a work of demolition, not construction.

In this passage, Ghazali responds to an objection by clarifying the nature of his book. He distinguishes between two approaches: foundation-laying and destruction. By framing his work as pure criticism, he exempts himself from the burden of offering alternative solutions. This lesson explores that distinction and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: an objection followed by Ghazali's response.
2
Identify the objection
The objection claims that Ghazali's own argument turns against him regarding God's knowledge. Find this part in the text.
The objection is introduced by 'فإنها' (for she said).
3
Understand Ghazali's distinction
Ghazali contrasts two roles: 'mumahhidin' (ground-layers) and 'hadimin mu'taridin' (destroyers/objectors). He aligns himself with the latter.
4
Connect to the title
This distinction explains why the book is called 'Tahafut al-Falasifa' (Incoherence of the Philosophers) – a work of refutation, not a grounding of truth.
5
Reflect on the implication
Because Ghazali is not presenting a positive doctrine, he is not obliged to answer the objection. This is a key methodological point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنها : إن هذا يقلب عليكم فى معلومات الله سبحانه وتعالى ، وأما قولهم غير متناهية ، والعلم عندكم واحد ، فنقول : نحن لم نخض في هذا الكتاب خوض الممهدين ، بل خوض الهادمين المعترضين ، ولذلك سمينا الكتاب تهافت الفلاسفة ( لا تمهيداً لحق ، فليس يلزمنا الجواب عن هذا
For she said: 'This turns back against you concerning the objects of knowledge of God Almighty, and as for their saying [they are] infinite, while knowledge according to you is one, we say: We did not engage in this book as ground-layers, but as destroyers and objectors. Therefore we named the book 'The Incoherence of the Philosophers', not a grounding of truth. So we are not obliged to answer this.'
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is a response to an objection that Ghazali's own argument about multiplicity of knowledge applies to God's knowledge as well, and that Muslims also believe God's knowledge is one. Ghazali clarifies the nature of his book. He says he is not writing as one who lays foundations (mumahhidīn) for a positive doctrine, but as a destroyer and objector (hādimīn mu‘tariḍīn). Hence the title 'Tahāfut al-Falāsifa' (Incoherence of the Philosophers), not a grounding (tamhīd) of truth. Therefore he is not obliged to provide a counter-answer to the objection. The phrase 'she said' likely refers to a hypothetical interlocutor personified as feminine in Arabic grammar. The point is that the book's purpose is purely critical: to show the inconsistencies of the philosophers, not to present and defend a specific theological system. Ghazali follows the order: first the objection, then his response identifying the book's method.

Key Terms
تهافت
ممهدين
هادمين
معترضين
تمهيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali's arguments in Tahafut commit him to a specific theological position.
Correction: Ghazali insists he is only providing a refutation, not grounding a truth, so he is not committed to the opposite view.

Checkpoint
Why does Ghazali say he is not obliged to answer the objection that the argument turned against him?
A) Because he has already answered it earlier
B) Because he is writing as a destroyer and objector, not a ground-layer
C) Because the objection is invalid
D) Because he does not care about consistency

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's Tahafut is a work of refutation, not construction—he seeks to demolish the philosophers' arguments without building an alternative.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you engage in discussions about faith, do you see yourself as a builder or a destroyer? How often do you criticize others' views without offering a constructive alternative, and what does that reveal about your intentions?
?
Ghazali openly admits he is not grounding truth in this work. Are you ever afraid to admit that you are only refuting, not building? In what areas of your life might you be hiding behind criticism to avoid the harder work of positive construction?
?
If God were to judge your debates, would He see you seeking truth or just winning arguments? This lesson shows that even a great scholar like Ghazali was clear about his limited role—how honest are you about the role you play in your own discussions?

Ghazali's Refutation of the Universal Objection and the Alternative of Revelation
Ghazali responds to an objection and presents a revelation-based alternative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali responds to the objection that his critique of philosophy is universally problematic, and how he presents reliance on revelation and miracles as an alternative to rational speculation in divine matters.

Core Idea
Reason has limits; revelation is the only path to divine truths.

In this passage, Ghazali addresses a potential counterargument: that the problem of multiplicity of knowledge applies to all sects, not just philosophers. He clarifies his real aim—to expose the philosophers' claim to certainty—and then points to a group that avoids rational speculation altogether, relying solely on revealed truth. This lesson explores his response and the alternative approach he presents.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and Ghazali's response
Start by reading the objection that the problem is universal, then see how Ghazali reframes his goal: to disable the philosophers, not solve every problem.
Notice the shift from solving to exposing.
2
Understand Ghazali's purpose
Ghazali states his purpose is to disable the philosophers' claim of certain proofs. This is key: he is not offering a new system but criticizing theirs.
3
Examine the alternative group described
Read the description of the group that relies on miracles, restricts reason, and follows revelation. Ghazali cites the hadith about pondering creation, not the essence of God.
This group is presented as a viable alternative.
4
Reflect on the final challenge
Ghazali ends by challenging the philosophers: they accuse this group of ignorance, yet their own proofs are not certain like geometry. Consider the contrast.
This undermines the philosophers' claim to superiority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : إنا لا نلزمكم مذهب فرقة معينة من الفرق ، فأما ما ينقلب على كافة فرق الخلق ، وتستوى الأقدام في إشكاله ، فلا يجوز لكم إيراده ، وهذا الإشكال منقلب عليكم ، ولا محيص لأحد من الفرق عنه قلنا : لا ، بل المقصود تعجيزكم عن دعواكم معرفة حقائق الأمور بالبراهين القطعية ، وتشكيككم فى دعواكم . وإذا ظهر عجزكم ، ففي الناس من يذهب إلى أن حقائق الأمور الإلهية ، لا تنال بنظر العقل ، بل ليس في قوة البشر الاطلاع عليها ، ولذلك قال صاحب الشرع - صلوات الله عليه : « تفكروا في خلق الله ، ولا تفكروا في ذات الله » فما إنكاركم على هذه الفرقة ؟ ! المعتقدة صدق الرسول - صلوات الله وسلامه عليه - بدليل المعجزة ، المقتصرة في قضية العقل ، على إثبات ذات المرسل ، المحترزة عن النظر في الصفات بنظر العقل المتبعة صاحب الشرع ، فيما أتى به من صفات الله تعالى ، المقتفية إثره ، في إطلاق « العالم ، والمريد ، والقادر ، والحى ، » ، المنتهية عن إطلاق ما لم يأذن فيه ، المعترفة بالعجز عن درك العقل حقيقته وإنما إنكاركم عليهم بنسبتهم إلى الجهل ، بمسالك البراهين ، ووجه ترتيب المقدمات ، على أشكال المقاييس ، ودعواكم أنا قد عرفنا ذلك بمسالك عقلية ، وقد بان عجزكم ، وتهافت مسالككم ، وافتضاحكم فى دعوى معرفتكم ، وهو هذا البيان ، فأين من يدعى أن براهين الإلهيات قاطعة كبراهين الهندسيات
If it is said: 'We do not impose on you the doctrine of a specific sect among the sects. As for what turns back against all sects of creation and stands equally problematic to all, it is not permissible for you to bring it up. And this problem turns back against you, and none of the sects can escape it.' We say: No. Rather, the purpose is to disable you from your claim of knowing the true natures of things by certain proofs, and to cast doubt on your claim. When your incapacity appears, among people are those who hold that the true natures of divine matters cannot be attained by intellectual speculation; rather, it is not within human power to grasp them. Therefore the Master of the Law (peace be upon him) said: 'Ponder the creation of God, and do not ponder the essence of God.' So why do you denounce this group? — those who believe the Messenger (peace and blessings be upon him) through the evidence of miracles, who restrict the role of reason to establishing the essence of the messenger, who refrain from investigating the attributes by reason, who follow the Master of the Law in what he reported of the attributes of God Almighty, who emulate him in affirming 'the Knowing, the Willing, the Powerful, the Living,' who stop at what he has not permitted, who confess the inability of reason to grasp His reality. And your denunciation of them is only by accusing them of ignorance of the ways of proofs, the manner of arranging premises in the forms of syllogisms, and your claim that we have known that through rational methods. Yet your incapacity has become evident, the incoherence of your methods, and your disgrace in claiming knowledge — and this is the exposition. So where is the one who claims that proofs of divine matters are certain like proofs of geometry?
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector says that the problem of multiplicity of knowledge affects all sects, not just the philosophers, so Ghazali should not bring it up. Ghazali responds by clarifying his goal: not to solve all problems, but to expose the philosophers' claim to have certain rational proofs (barāhīn qaṭ‘iyya). He then points to an alternative approach: a group that relies on revelation and miracles, restricting reason to establishing the prophet's veracity and accepting revealed attributes without rational investigation. Ghazali cites the hadith 'Ponder the creation of God, and do not ponder the essence of God' to support this. He then taunts the philosophers: they accuse this group of ignorance of logical methods, yet the philosophers themselves have been shown to be incapable, and their proofs are not certain like geometry proofs. The passage follows the order: objection, Ghazali's answer, description of the revealed group, and final challenge.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali is endorsing total rejection of reason in divine matters.
Correction: Ghazali is not endorsing total rejection; he is pointing to those who do so as an alternative to the philosophers' rational claims. He himself uses rational arguments elsewhere.

Checkpoint
According to the hadith cited by Ghazali, what are people commanded to ponder?
A) The essence of God
B) The attributes of God
C) The creation of God
D) The miracles of the prophets

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's aim is to expose the inadequacy of philosophical proof, not to solve all problems, and he presents reliance on revelation as a valid alternative.

?

?
The philosophers claimed certainty but were shown incapable. Are you honest about the limits of your own understanding of divine matters, or do you pretend to have certainty where revelation has not spoken?
?
Ghazali's group confesses reason's inability to grasp God's essence. Are you willing to stop at what God has revealed about Himself, or do you try to rationalize what He has not permitted? How will you check your heart's tendency to go beyond the limits?

The Orthodox Method: Reason's Role and Revelation's Authority
Ghazali describes the orthodox method of epistemic humility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari method of limiting reason to authenticating the messenger and relying on revelation for knowledge of divine attributes.

Core Idea
Reason authenticates the messenger; revelation teaches us about God.

After demonstrating the philosophers' incapacity, Ghazali presents an alternative approach: some scholars hold that divine realities are beyond unaided reason. They use reason only to verify the Prophet through miracles, then accept all attributes he taught. This lesson explains that method.

Guided Path
1
Identify the group Ghazali describes
Read the passage starting from 'ففي الناس' to 'العجز عن درك العقل حقيقته'. Notice how Ghazali describes their epistemic stance.
They are the orthodox scholars who prioritize revelation.
2
List the key principles of their method
Find where Ghazali enumerates their approach: (1) belief in the messenger through miracles, (2) limiting reason to proving the messenger, (3) refraining from rational speculation on attributes, (4) following the Lawgiver in affirming specific attributes, (5) acknowledging reason's incapacity.
These are the five components of their method.
3
Reflect on the role of reason vs. revelation
Ask yourself: Why do they limit reason? How does this contrast with the philosophers? The core idea is that reason serves revelation, not replaces it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القطعية ، وتشكيككم فى دعواكم . وإذا ظهر عجزكم ، ففي الناس من يذهب إلى أن حقائق الأمور الإلهية ، لا تنال بنظر العقل ، بل ليس في قوة البشر الاطلاع عليها ، ولذلك قال صاحب الشرع - صلوات الله عليه : « تفكروا في خلق الله ، ولا تفكروا في ذات الله » فما إنكاركم على هذه الفرقة ؟ ! المعتقدة صدق الرسول - صلوات الله وسلامه عليه - بدليل المعجزة ، المقتصرة في قضية العقل ، على إثبات ذات المرسل ، المحترزة عن النظر في الصفات بنظر العقل المتبعة صاحب الشرع ، فيما أتى به من صفات الله تعالى ، المقتفية إثره ، في إطلاق « العالم ، والمريد ، والقادر ، والحى ، » ، المنتهية عن إطلاق ما لم يأذن فيه ، المعترفة بالعجز عن درك العقل حقيقته وإنما إنكاركم عليهم بنسبتهم إلى الجهل ، بمسالك البراهين ، ووجه ترتيب المقدمات ، على أشكال المقاييس ، ودعواكم أنا قد عرفنا ذلك بمسالك عقلية ، وقد بان عجزكم ، وتهافت مسالككم ، وافتضاحكم فى دعوى معرفتكم ، وهو هذا البيان ، فأين من يدعى أن براهين الإلهيات قاطعة كبراهين الهندسيات .
The certainty, and your casting doubt on your claim. When your incapacity became apparent, there are among people those who hold that the realities of divine matters cannot be attained by intellect, rather humans do not have the power to investigate them. For this reason, the Lawgiver (peace be upon him) said: 'Reflect on the creation of God, and do not reflect on the essence of God.' So why do you deny this sect? The one who believes in the truthfulness of the Messenger (peace and blessings be upon him) through the evidence of the miracle, limiting the role of reason to proving the essence of the messenger, refraining from examining attributes by reason, following the Lawgiver in what he brought concerning the attributes of God most high, following his example in affirming 'the Knower, the Willer, the Powerful, the Living,' and refraining from affirming what he has not permitted, acknowledging reason's incapacity to grasp their reality. Your denial of them is only through attributing ignorance of the ways of proofs, the manner of arranging premises according to the forms of syllogisms, and your claim that we have known that by rational methods, and your incapacity has become clear, and your methods are inconsistent, and your disgrace in claiming knowledge is evident, and this is the demonstration. So where is the one who claims that proofs in divine matters are as certain as geometric proofs?
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage is a combined description and challenge. Ghazali first describes a group (clearly the Ash'aris or a similar orthodox faction) who respond to the philosophers' failure by adopting a position of epistemic humility in divine matters. They argue that human reason cannot independently grasp the realities of divine attributes, so they rely on revelation. They use reason only to authenticate the messenger through miracles, then accept all attributes the Prophet taught, such as 'Knower,' 'Willer,' 'Powerful,' 'Living,' and refrain from attributing anything God has not permitted. Ghazali then directly challenges the philosophers: 'Your denial of them is only through attributing ignorance of the ways of proofs, the manner of arranging premises according to the forms of syllogisms.' He argues that the philosophers' own rational methods have proven inconsistent, and their claim to certainty in divine matters is bogus, unlike the certainty of geometric proofs. Ghazali is defending the orthodox method by turning the tables on the philosophers: they accuse others of ignorance, but their own arguments are incoherent.

Key Terms
صاحب الشرع
معجزة
ذات المرسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Ghazali rejects reason entirely in divine matters.
Correction: Ghazali does not reject reason; he limits its role to authenticating the messenger and then follows revelation for attributes.

Checkpoint
According to the group described by Ghazali, what is the proper role of reason in divine matters?
A) Reason can independently discover all divine truths.
B) Reason is used only to prove the messenger via miracles, then attributes are accepted from revelation.
C) Reason is completely useless and should be abandoned.
D) Reason and revelation are in constant conflict.

Key Takeaway
The orthodox method uses reason to authenticate the messenger, then relies entirely on revelation for knowledge of God's attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a teaching from revelation that seems irrational to you, do you instinctively trust your own reasoning or submit to the authority of the messenger? Which attitude does Ghazali describe as orthodox? Ask yourself honestly: have you ever rejected a religious teaching because it didn't make sense to you? Will you now accept it even without rational explanation?
?
You use reason to prove the messenger's truthfulness—but do you then allow reason to dictate what God must be like? Examine your heart: are there attributes of God you affirm or deny based on your own logic rather than on what the Prophet taught? Will you commit to only affirming what revelation has permitted?
?
The orthodox method ends with admitting reason's incapacity. Is there an area of your faith where you secretly believe you can fully understand God's essence or attributes? How does pride in your intellectual abilities prevent you from true submission? Write down one thing about God that you struggle to accept on faith alone, and decide today to embrace it as a matter of worship.

Challenging the Philosophers' Claim to Certainty
Ghazali challenges the philosophers' claim to rational certainty

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's critique of the philosophers' claim that their rational proofs in divine matters are as certain as geometric proofs.

Core Idea
The philosophers' certainty in divine matters is false; their methods are inconsistent.

After describing the orthodox method, Ghazali turns the tables on the philosophers. He argues that their denial of the orthodox is based on accusations of ignorance, yet their own rational methods have proven incoherent. Far from achieving certainty, their arguments are exposed as weak. This lesson unpacks that challenge.

Guided Path
1
Read Ghazali's direct address to the philosophers
Read from 'وإنما إنكاركم عليهم' to the end. Note the accusatory tone and the logical structure.
He is directly challenging their claim.
2
Identify the components of his challenge
Ghazali lists: their denial is based on accusing others of ignorance of logic; they claim to know by rational methods; but their incapacity is clear; their methods are inconsistent; they are disgraced in claiming knowledge. Then he concludes with a rhetorical question comparing divine proofs to geometric proofs.
3
Reflect on the comparison with geometry
Why does Ghazali contrast with geometry? Because geometric proofs are universally certain. Their divine proofs are not. This highlights the weakness of their rational theology.

Prophetic Knowledge: Direct Divine Teaching vs. Philosophical Mechanism
Ghazali objects that knowledge of the unseen can come directly from Allah or an angel, without the philosophers' mechanism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's objection that prophetic knowledge of the unseen comes directly from Allah or an angel, without needing the philosophers' complex mechanism.

Core Idea
Prophetic knowledge is a gift from Allah, not a product of rational machinery.

In this passage, Ghazali responds to the philosophers' elaborate theory of prophecy by offering a simpler explanation: Allah directly teaches the prophet, or an angel does. He then challenges their misuse of religious texts and the limits of reason, asserting that such matters are known only by revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice Ghazali's direct and polemical tone.
2
Identify Ghazali's alternative explanation
Find where Ghazali states that prophetic and dream knowledge come from Allah's direct teaching or an angel. Compare it to the philosophers' mechanism of celestial souls and the Preserved Tablet.
Ghazali uses the term 'ibti'da' (primary creation) to emphasize directness.
3
Examine his critique of textual and rational arguments
Note how Ghazali first dismisses the philosophers' appeal to religious texts (Shari'ah) about the Tablet and Pen, then questions the sufficiency of their rational argument even if possible.
He concludes that the proper way is from revelation, not reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب، أن نقول: بم تنكرون على من يقول: إن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم يعرف الغيب بتعريف الله عز وجل على سبيل الابتداء، وكذا من يرى في المنام، فإنما يعرفه بتعريف الله تعالى أو بتعريف ملك من الملائكة، فلا يحتاج إلى شيء مما ذكرتموه، فلا دليل في هذا، ولا دليل لكم في ورود الشرع باللوح المحفوظ والقلم، فإن أهل الشرع لم يفهموا من اللوح والقلم هذا المعنى قطعاً، فلا متمسك لكم في الشرعيات. ويبقى التمسك بمسالك العقول، وما ذكرتموه وإن اعترف بإمكانه، مهما لم يشترط نفي النهاية عن هذه المعلومات، فلا يعرف وجوده، ولا يتحقق كذبه، وإنما السبيل فيه أن يتعرف من الشرع لا من العقل.
And the answer is that we say: Why do you deny one who says that the Prophet (peace and blessings be upon him) knows the unseen by the definition (ta'rif) of Allah Almighty, as a primary creation, and likewise the one who sees in a dream only knows it by the definition of Allah Most High or by the definition of an angel from the angels? So he does not need anything of what you have mentioned. So there is no proof in this, and you have no proof in the occurrence of the law (shar') regarding the Preserved Tablet and the Pen, for the people of the law (ahl al-shar') did not understand this meaning from the Tablet and the Pen at all. So you have no support in the legal matters. And the appeal remains to the paths of the intellects, and what you have mentioned, even if its possibility is admitted, as long as the condition of infinity is not negated for these items of knowledge, then its actual existence is not known, nor is its falsehood verified. But the way in this matter is to learn from the law (shar') not from the intellect.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This is Ghazali's direct response (jawab) to the philosophers' exposition. He first offers an alternative: simply attribute prophetic and dream knowledge to Allah's direct teaching or to an angel. This is simpler and does not require the elaborate machinery of celestial souls and Preserved Tablet. He then attacks their claim to have support from religious texts (shar'iyyat) about the Tablet and Pen; the religious scholars (ahl al-shar') did not understand them in this philosophical sense. Without textual support, their argument must rely purely on reason. But even if the possibility is conceded, as long as they do not deny the infinity of such knowledge, their argument does not prove actual existence or falsity. The proper way to know such matters is by revelation, not reason. Ghazali is thus defending the Ash'ari position that knowledge of the unseen is from Allah alone and cannot be deduced by human reason.

القلم
أهل الشرع
مسالك العقول
إمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali rejects all rational arguments entirely.
Correction: Ghazali only rejects this specific rational argument about the mechanism of prophecy; he does not reject reason per se, but limits its scope here.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, how does the prophet know the unseen?
A) Through connection to celestial souls.
B) Through his own reason.
C) Through Allah's direct definition (ta'rif) or an angel.
D) Through reading the Preserved Tablet.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali argues that prophetic knowledge of the unseen is directly from Allah or an angel, not via the philosophers' mechanism, and that such matters are known only through revelation.

?

Where does your certainty actually lie? Will you submit your intellect to the text even when it seems 'unreasonable'?
?
When you encounter a matter of the unseen that reason cannot prove—such as the nature of angelic communication—do you accept it with full conviction, or do you demand a rational proof before believing? What does your stance reveal about the state of your heart towards revelation?
?
Have you ever quietly questioned a prophetic tradition about the unseen because it clashed with your sense of what is reasonable? If so, what would Ghazali say to you about the proper source for such knowledge? Are you willing to let the law (shar') define reality for you, even at the cost of your intellectual comfort?

Ghazali's Strategy: Contesting Three Premises
Ghazali introduces three contested premises, starting with the voluntary motion of heaven.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali strategically narrows the debate by contesting only three key premises from the philosophers' rational proof, beginning with the claim that the heavens move voluntarily.

Core Idea
Ghazali wins arguments by isolating and refuting key premises.

Ghazali begins his detailed critique by stating that the philosophers' rational proof rests on many premises. He will not refute all, but contests only three. The first premise is the claim that heaven's motion is voluntary (iradiyya). He asserts that this claim has already been invalidated in a previous discussion, thus cutting off the foundation of their argument. This passage shows Ghazali's surgical approach to dismantling complex arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get the gist of Ghazali's approach.
2
Identify Ghazali's strategy
Notice that Ghazali does not try to refute every premise. He selects three critical ones. Why is this effective?
3
Focus on the first premise
The first premise is that the heavens move voluntarily. Ghazali says he has already refuted it. Consider how this affects the philosophers' argument.
This premise is crucial for their theory of celestial souls.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما ذكرتموه من الدليل العقلي أولاً، فمبنى على مقدمات كثيرة، لسنا نطول بإبطالها، ولكنا ننازع في ثلاث مقدمات منها. المقدمة الأولى، قولكم: إن حركة السماء إرادية، وقد فرغنا من هذه المسألة وإبطال دعواكم فيها.

The first premise: your statement that the motion of the heaven is voluntary (iradiyya). We have already finished with this issue and the invalidation of your claim concerning it.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
In this short passage, Ghazali begins his detailed critique of the philosophers' rational proof for knowledge of the unseen (which was based on the celestial souls knowing causes). He says the proof has many premises, and he only needs to contest three. The first is that the heavens move voluntarily. He claims he has already refuted this in a previous discussion (likely in an earlier part of Tahafut). By attacking this premise, he undermines the foundation of their argument that celestial souls are volitional agents that can impart knowledge. This is a typical Ghazalian strategy: isolate key premises and refute them.

Refuting the Third Premise: The Absurdity of Knowing All Consequences
Ghazali dismisses the claim that knowledge of motion entails knowledge of all its details as arbitrary and absurd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali considers the claim that knowledge of motion entails knowledge of all its consequences to be an arbitrary and absurd premise.

Core Idea
Knowing a motion does not require knowing every trivial detail it causes.

The philosophers claimed that if you conceive a particular motion, you must also conceive all its consequences and necessary concomitants. Ghazali dismisses this as pure arbitrariness and gives a vivid, almost comical example to show its absurdity. As you read the passage, notice how he uses a concrete everyday scenario to expose the unreasonableness of the premise.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage once
Read the Arabic passage without trying to analyze deeply. Let Ghazali's tone and the structure of his refutation come through. Notice how he labels the premise as 'extremely arbitrary'.
The passage uses sarcasm to highlight absurdity.
2
Identify the philosophers' claim
Locate the precise claim being refuted: that if one conceives particular motions, one also conceives their consequences and necessary concomitants.
This is the third premise Ghazali is attacking.
3
Unpack the example
Follow the detailed example of a person walking in the sun. Ghazali lists an array of consequences: shadow positions, cooling effects, earth compression, bodily changes. Ask yourself: is it reasonable to expect someone to know all this just from knowing they are walking?
The example is deliberately extended to show how ridiculous the premise is.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Ghazali concludes that this is 'evident nonsense' that no sane person would imagine. Understand that he is not just saying it's impractical—he is saying it's logically and rationally impossible.
This refutation is a form of reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثالثة : وهى التحكم البعيد جداً ، قولهم : إنه إذا تصور الحركات الجزئية ، تصوّر أيضاً ، توابعها ولوازمها ، وهذا هوس محض ، كقول القائل : إن الإنسان إذا تحرك ، وعرف حركته ، ينبغى أن يعرف ، ما يلزم من حركته ، من موازاة ومجاوزة ، وهو نسبته إلى الأجسام ، التي فوقه وتحته ومن جوانبه ، وإنه إذا مشى في شمس ، ينبغى أن يعلم المواضع ، التي يقع عليها ظله ، والمواضع التي لا يقع عليها ، وما يحصل عن ظله ، من البرودة ، بقطع الشعاع في تلك المواضع ، وما يحصل من الانضغاط لأجزاء الأرض تحت قدمه ، وما يحصل من التفريق فيها ، وما يحصل فى أخلاطه فى الباطن ، من الاستحالة ، بسبب الحركة ، إلى الحرارة وما يستحيل من أجزائه إلى العرق ، وهلم جرا ، إلى جميع الحواث ، في بدنه وفى غير بدنه ، مما الحركة علة فيه ، أو شرط ، أو مهئ ومعد ، وهو هوس بين، لا يتخيله عاقل ، ولا يغتر به إلا جاهل، وإلى هذا ، يرجع هذا التحكم .
The third premise: and it is an extremely arbitrary claim, their statement: 'If he conceives the particular motions, he also conceives their consequences and necessary concomitants.' This is pure nonsense, like someone saying: if a person moves and knows his motion, he must know what follows from his motion, such as parallelism and adjacency, and his relation to the bodies above, below, and around him; and if he walks in the sun, he must know the places where his shadow falls and where it does not, and what results from his shadow of coolness by cutting off the rays in those places, and what results from the compression of the parts of the earth under his foot, and what results from the dispersion in them, and what results in his internal humors from transformation due to motion into heat, and what transforms from his parts into sweat, and so on to all the events in his body and outside his body, of which the motion is a cause, or a condition, or a preparation and disposition. This is evident nonsense that no sane person would imagine, and none but the ignorant would be deceived by it. And to this reduces this arbitrary claim.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the philosophers' third premise. Ghazali calls it an extremely arbitrary claim: the idea that if one conceives particular motions, one also conceives all their consequences and necessary concomitants. He declares this pure nonsense and gives a sarcastic example: if a person walks in the sun, according to this premise he would need to know exactly where his shadow falls, the cooling effect, the compression of earth under his feet, the changes in his humors, sweat, and countless other effects of which the motion is a cause, condition, or preparation. Ghazali concludes that this is obvious nonsense that no sane person would accept, and that the premise reduces to this absurdity.

هوس

لوازم

Misconception: Some might think that if you know an action, you should automatically know all its physical effects and details.
Correction: Ghazali demonstrates through the walking-in-the-sun example that this is impossible and absurd; knowledge of a motion does not entail knowledge of every trivial consequence.

Checkpoint

The claim that knowledge of a motion includes knowledge of all its consequences is arbitrary nonsense, as proven by the absurdity of the required details in everyday actions like walking.

Classification of Natural Sciences into Eight Principles
Ghazali classifies the natural sciences into eight principles and introduces his selective approach.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's classification of the natural sciences into eight principles and his general stance that Islamic law does not oppose them except in specific points.

Core Idea
Natural philosophy is mostly acceptable; only specific conclusions conflict with revelation.

In this passage from the Tahafut, Ghazali outlines the classification of natural sciences (al-ṭabī‘iyyāt) as understood by the philosophers. He begins by clarifying that Islamic law does not require rejection of these sciences wholesale, only certain doctrines. Then he lists the eight principles, describing the first six with their corresponding Aristotelian books. This orientation is crucial for understanding where Ghazali's critique will be selective.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then the translation. Notice the overall structure: an introduction followed by a numbered list.
The passage is dense with technical terms; don't worry about memorising them yet.
2
Identify Ghazali's stance on natural sciences
Find the sentence where Ghazali says the Law does not require disputation or rejection except in specific places. This is key to his methodology.
3
List the eight principles (as far as given)
The passage mentions eight principles but describes only six. List each of the six as they appear, noting the book references.
The seventh and eighth are not included in this excerpt.
4
Note the correspondence with Aristotelian books
Each principle is linked to a book title (e.g., 'Sam‘ al-Kiyān' for Physics). Recognise that Ghazali is summarising the Aristotelian corpus.
These Arabic titles refer to Aristotle's works.
5
Reflect on the principles vs. branches distinction
Ghazali divides natural sciences into principles (uṣūl) and branches (furū‘). Consider why this distinction matters for his critique.
He will challenge some specific doctrines (branches) but accept the framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الملقبة بالطبيعيات فهي علوم كثيرة نذكر أقسامها، ليعرف أن الشرع، ليس يقتضي المنازعة فيها، ولا إنكارها إلا في مواضع ذكرناها. وهي منقسمة إلى أصول وفروع وأصولها ثمانية أقسام: الأول: يذكر فيه ما يلحق الجسم، من حيث إنه جسم، من الانقسام والحركة، والتغير، وما يلحق الحركة ويتبعها، من الزمان، والمكان والخلاء، ويشتمل عليه كتاب «سمع الكيان». الثاني: يعرف أحوال أقسام أركان العالم، التي هي السموات، وما في مقعر فلك القمر، من العناصر الأربعة، وطبائعها، وعلة استحقاق كل واحد منها، موضوعاً معيناً، ويشتمل عليه، كتاب «السماء والعالم السفلى». الثالث: يعرف فيه، أحوال الكون والفساد، والتولد، والتوالد، والنشوء والبيلى، والاستحالات، وكيفية استبقاء الأنواع، على فساد الأشخاص، بالحركتين السماويتين، الشرقية والغربية، ويشتمل عليه، كتاب «الكون والفساد». الرابع: فى الأحوال التي تعرض، للعناصر الأربعة، من الامتزاجات، التي منها تحدث الآثار العلوية، من الغيوم، والأمطار، والرعد، والبرق، والهالة، وقوس قزح، والصواعق؛ والرياح، والزلازل. الخامس: فى الجواهر المعدنية. السادس: في أحكام النبات
As for what is called the natural sciences, they are numerous sciences. We will mention their divisions, so that it may be known that the Law does not require disputation in them, nor rejection of them, except in places we have mentioned. They are divided into principles and branches. Their principles are eight divisions: First: It mentions what pertains to the body, insofar as it is a body, of division, motion, change, and what pertains to motion and follows it, of time, place, and void. The book 'Sam‘ al-Kiyān' covers it. Second: It defines the states of the divisions of the pillars of the world, which are the heavens and what is in the concavity of the lunar sphere, of the four elements, their natures, and the cause of each one deserving a specific subject. The book 'al-Samā' wa al-‘Ālam al-Suflī' covers it. Third: It defines the states of generation and corruption, procreation, reproduction, growth, decay, transformations, and how species are preserved despite the corruption of individuals, by the two celestial motions, eastern and western. The book 'al-Kawn wa al-Fasād' covers it. Fourth: Concerning the states that occur to the four elements, of mixtures, from which arise the higher effects: clouds, rain, thunder, lightning, halo, rainbow, thunderbolts, winds, and earthquakes. Fifth: Concerning mineral substances. Sixth: Concerning the rules of plants
Source pages p. 233

Commentary
This passage is a classification and introduction to the natural sciences (al-ṭabī‘iyyāt) as treated by the philosophers. Ghazali begins by stating that these sciences are numerous, but he will outline their divisions to show that Islamic law (al-shar‘) does not require opposition or rejection of them, except for specific points he has previously indicated. This is an important methodological stance: natural philosophy is largely acceptable; only certain conclusions (like those contradicting revelation) are contested. He then divides these sciences into principles (uṣūl) and branches (furū‘). The principles are eight, and he describes the first six: (1) The study of the body as such, including division, motion, change, and the concomitants of motion: time, place, and void. This corresponds to the book 'Sam‘ al-Kiyān' (Physics). (2) The states of the celestial spheres and the sublunary realm, including the four elements, their natures, and why each element occupies its natural place. This is covered in 'al-Samā' wa al-‘Ālam al-Suflī' (On the Heavens and the World). (3) Generation and corruption, reproduction, growth, decay, transformations, and how species persist through individual corruption due to the two celestial motions. This is the book 'al-Kawn wa al-Fasād' (On Generation and Corruption). (4) The mixtures of the four elements that produce meteorological phenomena: clouds, rain, thunder, lightning, halo, rainbow, etc. (5) Mineral substances. (6) The rules of plants (botany). The listed books are likely Aristotle's works as translated into Arabic. The passage breaks off after the sixth, but the original plan is eight, indicating that the seventh and eighth would cover animals and perhaps the soul. This classification shows Ghazali's familiarity with Aristotelian natural philosophy and his selective critique of it.

Misconception: Some may think Ghazali rejects all natural sciences outright as contrary to Islam.
Correction: Ghazali explicitly states that the Law does not require disputation or rejection of these sciences, except in specific places he has indicated. He accepts the overall framework.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
