Introduction to Natural Sciences and Shari'ah
Ghazali introduces the natural sciences and delimits the scope of Shari'ah's disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali enumerates the natural sciences and how he positions them relative to Islamic law.

Core Idea
Shari'ah does not reject natural sciences—only specific philosophical claims.

Ghazali begins by clarifying his intention: he lists the natural sciences not to reject them wholesale, but to identify where philosophy oversteps religious boundaries. This lesson sets the stage for his targeted critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening statement
Read the first Arabic sentence noting Ghazali's claim that Shari'ah does not require dispute or denial of natural sciences except in specific places.
This is a key methodological point.
2
Distinguish principles from branches
Notice the division into principles (uṣūl) and branches (furūʿ). This is a standard classification in Islamic sciences.
3
Reflect on the theological motivation
Consider why Ghazali makes this statement. He wants to establish common ground before critiquing specific philosophical doctrines.
This helps avoid the misconception that he rejects all natural philosophy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الملقبة بالطبيعيات فهي علوم كثيرة نذكر أقسامها ليعرف أن الشرع ليس يقتضي المنازعة فيها ولا إنكارها إلا في مواضع ذكرناها. وهي منقسمة إلى أصول وفروع. وأصولها ثمانية أقسام: الأول: يذكر فيه ما يلحق الجسم من حيث إنه جسم من الانقسام والحركة والتغير وما يلحق الحركة ويتبعها من الزمان والمكان والخلاء ويشتمل عليه كتاب «سمع الكيان». الثاني: يعرف أحوال أقسام أركان العالم التي هي السموات وما في مقعر فلك القمر من العناصر الأربعة وطبائعها وعلة استحقاق كل واحد منها موضوعاً معيناً ويشتمل عليه كتاب «السماء والعالم السفلى». الثالث: يعرف فيه أحوال الكون والفساد والتولد والتوالد والنشوء والبيلى والاستحالات وكيفية استبقاء الأنواع على فساد الأشخاص بالحركتين السماويتين الشرقية والغربية ويشتمل عليه كتاب «الكون والفساد». الرابع: في الأحوال التي تعرض للعناصر الأربعة من الامتزاجات التي منها تحدث الآثار العلوية من الغيوم والأمطار والرعد والبرق والهالة وقوس قزح والصواعق والرياح والزلازل. الخامس: في الجواهر المعدنية. السادس: في أحكام النبات. السابع: في الحيوانات وفيه كتاب «طبائع الحيوان». الثامن: في النفس الحيوانية والقوى الداركة وأن النفس الإنسانية لا تموت بموت البدن وأنه جوهر روحاني يستحيل عليه الفناء
As for that which is called the natural sciences, they are many sciences. We mention their divisions so that it is known that the Shari'ah does not require dispute in them nor their denial except in places we have mentioned. They are divided into principles and branches. Their principles are eight divisions: First: It mentions what attaches to body, insofar as it is body, of division, motion, and change, and what attaches to motion and follows it of time, place, and void, and it is contained in the book 'Samʿ al-Kiyān'. Second: It teaches the states of the divisions of the pillars of the world, which are the heavens and what is in the concavity of the sphere of the moon of the four elements, their natures, and the cause of each one deserving a specific subject, and it is contained in the book 'The Heavens and the Sublunary World'. Third: It teaches therein the states of generation and corruption, generation and reproduction, growth and decay, transformations, and how species are preserved despite the corruption of individuals by the two celestial motions, the eastern and the western, and it is contained in the book 'Generation and Corruption'. Fourth: Concerning the states that occur to the four elements from mixtures from which arise the upper effects of clouds, rain, thunder, lightning, halo, rainbow, thunderbolts, winds, and earthquakes. Fifth: Concerning mineral substances. Sixth: Concerning the laws of plants. Seventh: Concerning animals, and in it is the book 'The Natures of Animals'. Eighth: Concerning the animal soul and the perceiving faculties, and that the human soul does not die with the death of the body, and that it is a spiritual substance upon which annihilation is impossible.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage classifies the natural sciences (al-ṭabīʿiyyāt) as understood by the philosophers. Ghazali begins by stating that these are many sciences, and his purpose in listing their divisions is to clarify that religious law (sharʿ) does not require disputing or denying them except in specific points he will later mention. He then divides them into principles (uṣūl) and branches (furūʿ). Here he enumerates eight principles. The first principle studies properties of body qua body, such as divisibility, motion, change, and attendant concepts of time, place, and void. The second covers the heavens and the sublunary world, including the four elements. The third deals with generation and corruption, and how species endure through celestial motions. The fourth concerns meteorological phenomena resulting from elemental mixtures. The fifth, sixth, and seventh treat minerals, plants, and animals respectively. The eighth principle discusses the animal soul and the human soul, asserting that the human soul is a spiritual substance that does not perish with the body. This last point is theologically significant and foreshadows Ghazali's later critique.

Correction: Ghazali explicitly states that Shari'ah does not require dispute or denial except in specific places he mentions.

Key Takeaway

First Two Principles: Body and the Heavens
Ghazali describes the first two principles of natural sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first two principles of natural sciences: the study of body qua body (division, motion, time, place, void) and the study of the heavens and the four elements.

Core Idea
Nature is studied through body's properties and the cosmic structure of heavens and elements.

Ghazali begins the enumeration of the eight principles. The first principle covers the intrinsic properties of body, such as divisibility and motion, along with their attendants like time and place. The second principle treats the celestial and sublunary realms, including the four elements and why each occupies its place. These form the foundational subjects of Aristotelian physics.

Guided Path
1
Study the first principle
Read the description of the first principle: what attaches to body as body, including division, motion, change, time, place, void. Note that this corresponds to Aristotle's Physics.
The Arabic title 'Samʿ al-Kiyān' is the standard name for Aristotle's Physics in the Islamic tradition.
2
Study the second principle
Read the second principle: the heavens and the sublunary world of four elements, their natures, and why each element has a specific place (e.g., fire upward, earth downward).
This corresponds to Aristotle's De Caelo et Mundo.
3
Compare the two
Notice that the first principle deals with universal properties of all bodies, while the second deals with the particular arrangement of the cosmos.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الملقبة بالطبيعيات فهي علوم كثيرة نذكر أقسامها ليعرف أن الشرع ليس يقتضي المنازعة فيها ولا إنكارها إلا في مواضع ذكرناها. وهي منقسمة إلى أصول وفروع. وأصولها ثمانية أقسام: الأول: يذكر فيه ما يلحق الجسم من حيث إنه جسم من الانقسام والحركة والتغير وما يلحق الحركة ويتبعها من الزمان والمكان والخلاء ويشتمل عليه كتاب «سمع الكيان». الثاني: يعرف أحوال أقسام أركان العالم التي هي السموات وما في مقعر فلك القمر من العناصر الأربعة وطبائعها وعلة استحقاق كل واحد منها موضوعاً معيناً ويشتمل عليه كتاب «السماء والعالم السفلى». الثالث: يعرف فيه أحوال الكون والفساد والتولد والتوالد والنشوء والبيلى والاستحالات وكيفية استبقاء الأنواع على فساد الأشخاص بالحركتين السماويتين الشرقية والغربية ويشتمل عليه كتاب «الكون والفساد». الرابع: في الأحوال التي تعرض للعناصر الأربعة من الامتزاجات التي منها تحدث الآثار العلوية من الغيوم والأمطار والرعد والبرق والهالة وقوس قزح والصواعق والرياح والزلازل. الخامس: في الجواهر المعدنية. السادس: في أحكام النبات. السابع: في الحيوانات وفيه كتاب «طبائع الحيوان». الثامن: في النفس الحيوانية والقوى الداركة وأن النفس الإنسانية لا تموت بموت البدن وأنه جوهر روحاني يستحيل عليه الفناء
As for that which is called the natural sciences, they are many sciences. We mention their divisions so that it is known that the Shari'ah does not require dispute in them nor their denial except in places we have mentioned. They are divided into principles and branches. Their principles are eight divisions: First: It mentions what attaches to body, insofar as it is body, of division, motion, and change, and what attaches to motion and follows it of time, place, and void, and it is contained in the book 'Samʿ al-Kiyān'. Second: It teaches the states of the divisions of the pillars of the world, which are the heavens and what is in the concavity of the sphere of the moon of the four elements, their natures, and the cause of each one deserving a specific subject, and it is contained in the book 'The Heavens and the Sublunary World'. Third: It teaches therein the states of generation and corruption, generation and reproduction, growth and decay, transformations, and how species are preserved despite the corruption of individuals by the two celestial motions, the eastern and the western, and it is contained in the book 'Generation and Corruption'. Fourth: Concerning the states that occur to the four elements from mixtures from which arise the upper effects of clouds, rain, thunder, lightning, halo, rainbow, thunderbolts, winds, and earthquakes. Fifth: Concerning mineral substances. Sixth: Concerning the laws of plants. Seventh: Concerning animals, and in it is the book 'The Natures of Animals'. Eighth: Concerning the animal soul and the perceiving faculties, and that the human soul does not die with the death of the body, and that it is a spiritual substance upon which annihilation is impossible.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage classifies the natural sciences (al-ṭabīʿiyyāt) as understood by the philosophers. Ghazali begins by stating that these are many sciences, and his purpose in listing their divisions is to clarify that religious law (sharʿ) does not require disputing or denying them except in specific points he will later mention. He then divides them into principles (uṣūl) and branches (furūʿ). Here he enumerates eight principles. The second covers the heavens and the sublunary world, including the four elements. The third deals with generation and corruption, and how species endure through celestial motions. The fourth concerns meteorological phenomena resulting from elemental mixtures. The fifth, sixth, and seventh treat minerals, plants, and animals respectively. The eighth principle discusses the animal soul and the human soul, asserting that the human soul is a spiritual substance that does not perish with the body. This last point is theologically significant and foreshadows Ghazali's later critique.

Principles Three to Seven: Generation, Meteorology, and Living Beings
Ghazali describes principles three to seven of natural sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third through seventh principles of natural sciences, covering generation and corruption, meteorological phenomena, minerals, plants, and animals.

Core Idea
Nature includes processes of change, weather, and the three kingdoms: mineral, plant, animal.

Ghazali continues listing the principles, moving from the general to the particular. The third principle deals with generation and corruption, how species persist despite individual decay. The fourth covers meteorological events like rain, thunder, and earthquakes. The fifth, sixth, and seventh treat minerals, plants, and animals respectively. These form the core of natural history in the Aristotelian framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the third principle
Focus on generation and corruption, and how species are preserved through celestial motions (east-west). This is from Aristotle's Generation and Corruption.
2
Read the fourth principle
Note the list of meteorological phenomena: clouds, rain, thunder, lightning, halo, rainbow, thunderbolts, winds, earthquakes. These arise from elemental mixtures.
This corresponds to Aristotle's Meteorology.
3
Read principles five through seven
These cover minerals, plants, and animals. The seventh includes a specific book 'The Natures of Animals' (likely Aristotle's History of Animals).

Eighth Principle: The Soul and Its Immortality
Ghazali describes the eighth principle on the soul and its immortality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the eighth principle of natural sciences: the animal soul, the human soul's immortality, and its nature as a spiritual substance.

Core Idea
The human soul is a spiritual substance that does not die with the body.

The eighth and final principle addresses the soul. Ghazali distinguishes between the animal soul and the human soul, asserting that the human soul does not perish with the body and is a spiritual substance (jawhar rūḥānī) incapable of annihilation. This principle is a key point of theological conflict with some philosophers, and Ghazali will later critique it.

Guided Path
1
Read the eighth principle
Notice the two parts: first, the animal soul and the perceiving faculties; second, the claim that the human soul does not die with the body and is a spiritual substance.
This is the most theologically charged principle.
2
Identify key terms
Focus on 'nafs insāniyya' (human soul), 'mawt al-badan' (death of the body), 'jawhar rūḥānī' (spiritual substance), and 'fanāʾ' (annihilation).
These terms are central to the debate.
3
Reflect on implications
Consider why Ghazali includes this principle under natural sciences: the philosophers treat the soul as a natural entity. Also, note that Ghazali will later argue against some claims about the soul.

?

The Seven Branches of Natural Sciences and the Four Points of Disagreement
Ghazali lists the seven branches of natural sciences and introduces four disputed issues.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seven branches of natural sciences as classified by Ghazali and the key point that Islamic law does not oppose them, only four specific issues.

Core Idea
Ghazali accepts all seven natural sciences, opposing only four philosophical claims.

In this passage, Ghazali enumerates the seven branches of natural sciences (furūʿ) that the philosophers study. He defines each briefly and then makes a crucial methodological statement: there is no religious opposition to these sciences themselves. Instead, the opposition is confined to four specific issues that he will address later. This sets the stage for the Tahāfut's critique, distinguishing between acceptable sciences and problematic philosophical doctrines.

Guided Path
1
Read the enumeration of branches
Read through the seven branches Ghazali lists: medicine, astrology, physiognomy, dream interpretation, talismans, enchantments, and alchemy. Notice how each is defined briefly.
2
Identify the purpose of each branch
For each branch, note Ghazali's description of its purpose or method. Pay special attention to how he characterizes astrology as 'conjecture' (takhmin).
3
Highlight the final sentence
The last sentence is critical: Ghazali states that there is no religious opposition in any of these sciences. Instead, opposition is limited to four issues. This separates the sciences themselves from the philosophical claims.
This sets up the entire Tahāfut project.
4
Reflect on the scope of disagreement
Consider why Ghazali would accept these sciences while rejecting certain doctrines. This distinction shows his nuanced approach: he does not reject all of Greek thought, only specific metaphysical claims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما فروعها فسبعة: الأول: الطب ومقصوده معرفة مبادئ بدن الإنسان وأحواله من الصحة والمرض وأسبابها ودلائلها ليدفع المرض وتحفظ الصحة. الثاني: في أحكام النجوم وهو تخمين في الاستدلال من الكواكب وامتزاجاتها على ما يكون من أحوال العالم والملك والمواليد والسنين. الثالث: علم الفراسة وهو استدلال من الخلق على الأخلاق. الرابع: التعبير وهو استدلال من المتخيلات الحلمية على ما شاهدته النفس من عالم الغيب فشبهته القوة المتخيلة بمثال غيره. الخامس: علم الطلسمات وهو تأليف القوى السماوية بقوى بعض الأجرام الأرضية لتتألف من ذلك قوة تفعل فعلاً غريباً في العالم الأرضي. السادس: علم النيرنجات وهو مزج قوى الجواهر الأرضية ليحدث منها أمور غريبة. السابع: علم الكيمياء ومقصوده تبديل خواص الجواهر المعدنية ليتوصل إلى تحصيل الذهب والفضة بنوع من الحيل. وليس يلزم مخالفتهم شرعاً في شيء من هذه العلوم، وإنما نخالفهم من حملة هذه العلوم في أربع مسائل.
As for its branches, they are seven: First: Medicine, and its purpose is to know the principles of the human body and its states of health and illness, their causes and signs, in order to repel illness and preserve health. Second: Concerning the judgments of the stars, which is conjecture in inference from the planets and their mixtures concerning what will be of the states of the world, kingship, births, and years. Third: The science of physiognomy (firāsa), which is inference from the creation (form) to the character. Fourth: Interpretation of dreams (taʿbīr), which is inference from imaginative dream images to what the soul witnessed of the unseen world, so the imaginative power represented it by a similitude of something else. Fifth: The science of talismans (ṭilasmāt), which is combining celestial powers with powers of some earthly bodies to produce from that a power that performs a strange action in the earthly world. Sixth: The science of enchantments (nīranjāt), which is mixing the powers of earthly substances to produce from them strange things. Seventh: The science of alchemy (kīmiyāʾ), and its purpose is to change the properties of mineral substances to achieve the acquisition of gold and silver by a type of artifice. And it is not required to oppose them religiously in any of these sciences. Rather, we oppose them, among the carriers of these sciences, in four issues.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage continues the classification of natural sciences by listing their seven branches (furūʿ). Ghazali enumerates each branch with a brief definition. The first is medicine, aimed at preserving health and treating disease. The second is astrology, which he characterizes as conjecture. The third is physiognomy, inferring character from physical form. The fourth is dream interpretation, involving the imaginative faculty. The fifth and sixth are talismans and enchantments, which mix celestial and earthly powers to produce extraordinary effects. The seventh is alchemy, seeking to transmute minerals into gold and silver. After listing these, Ghazali makes a crucial methodological statement: Islamic law does not require opposing the philosophers in any of these sciences. Rather, opposition is confined to four specific issues (masāʾil), which he will address in the following discussion. This sets up the central polemic of the Tahāfut, where Ghazali will critique the philosophers on select metaphysical and theological points.

Key Terms
فروع
طب
أحكام النجوم
فراسة
تعبير
طلسمات
نيرنجات
كيمياء
مسائل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, in which of the following sciences is religious opposition required?
A) Medicine
B) Astrology
C) Alchemy
D) None of these sciences require religious opposition

Key Takeaway
Ghazali accepts the empirical/practical aspects of the seven natural sciences and reserves opposition for four philosophical issues.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ghazali does not reject these sciences outright; he distinguishes between the empirical and the philosophical. Is there an area of knowledge you have dismissed because it came from a source you distrust, without first separating its useful content from its worldview? Will you examine your own biases honestly today?
?
You have just read that Ghazali accepts medicine, physiognomy, and even alchemy as legitimate. But the four disputed issues are not listed here. Are you curious enough to search out what they are, or are you content to remain in comfortable ignorance? What will you do to find the truth?
?
If Ghazali can humbly accept seven sciences from the philosophers, what does your own attitude toward secular knowledge reveal about your humility or arrogance? Are you willing to learn from anyone, or do you reject knowledge simply because of its source? Repent and open your heart.

The Seven Branches of Natural Sciences According to Ghazali
Ghazali enumerates the seven branches of natural sciences and defines each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seven branches of the natural sciences as enumerated by Imam Ghazali, along with the purpose of each.

Core Idea
Ghazali identifies seven distinct sciences within the natural philosophy of his time.

In this passage, Imam al-Ghazali provides a clear classification of the natural sciences as understood by the philosophers. He lists seven branches, each with a specific focus, ranging from medicine to alchemy. This enumeration helps us see the breadth of inquiry the philosophers considered part of 'natural science'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get a feel for Ghazali's style. Then read the literal translation to understand each branch's definition.
Notice that each branch is introduced with a number in the Arabic.
2
Identify each branch and its purpose
Go through each of the seven branches. For each, note what Ghazali says is its purpose or method. Write them down in your own words.
Some branches use the word 'istidlal' (inference) – look for that pattern.
3
Reflect on the pattern of inference
Many of these sciences are described as types of inference (istidlal). Consider what kind of evidence each uses (e.g., from body to health, from stars to events, from appearance to character, from dreams to unseen).
This reflects the philosophical method of reasoning from observable signs.
4
Consider the orc and occult sciences
Ghazali includes astrology, talismans, and alchemy. Think about why these were considered 'natural sciences' in his time. Are they all equally empirical?
The passage does not evaluate their validity – that comes later.
5
Address the common misunderstanding
Read the common misunderstanding note: Ghazali is not endorsing all these sciences; he is simply describing what the philosophers claim. His own critique will follow.
Keep this in mind to avoid misinterpreting his stance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما فروعها فسبعة :
الأول : الطب ، ومقصوده معرفة مبادئ بدن الإنسان وأحواله من الصحة والمرض وأسبابها ودلائلها ، ليدفع المرض وتحفظ الصحة .
الثاني : في أحكام النجوم ، وهو تخمين في الاستدلال من الكواكب وامتزاجاتها على ما يكون من أحوال العالم والملك والمواليد والسنين .
الثالث : علم الفراسة ، وهو استدلال من الخلق على الأخلاق .
الرابع : التعبير ، وهو استدلال من المتخيلات الحلمية على ما شاهدته النفس من عالم الغيب ، فشبهته القوة المتخيلة بمثال غيره .
الخامس : علم الطلسمات ، وهو تأليف القوى السماوية بقوى بعض الأجرام الأرضية لتتألف من ذلك قوة تفعل فعلاً غريباً في العالم الأرضي .
السادس : علم النيرنجات ، وهو مزج قوى الجواهر الأرضية ليحدث منها أمور غريبة .
السابع : علم الكيمياء ، ومقصوده تبديل خواص الجواهر المعدنية ليتوصل إلى تحصيل الذهب والفضة بنوع من الحيل .
As for its branches, they are seven: First: Medicine, and its purpose is knowledge of the principles of the human body and its states of health and illness, their causes and indicators, to repel illness and preserve health. Second: The judgments of the stars, which is a conjectural inference from the planets and their mixtures about what will happen in the conditions of the world, kingship, births, and years. Third: Physiognomy, which is inference from the outward form to moral character. Fourth: Dream interpretation, which is inference from dream images to what the soul witnessed from the unseen world, so the imaginative faculty represented it by an example of something else. Fifth: The science of talismans, which is combining celestial powers with powers of some earthly bodies to produce from that a power that performs a strange act in the earthly world. Sixth: The science of al-niranjat, which is mixing powers of earthly substances to produce from them strange things. Seventh: Alchemy, and its purpose is to change the properties of mineral substances to attain gold and silver by a kind of artifice.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of the seven branches of the natural sciences. Ghazali states that the natural sciences have seven branches, then lists each one with a brief definition of its purpose. The first is medicine, concerned with the principles of the human body and maintaining health. The second is astrology, which makes conjectural inferences from celestial bodies about worldly events. The third is physiognomy, inferring character from physical appearance. The fourth is dream interpretation, inferring from dream images what the soul perceived from the unseen world. The fifth is talismans, combining celestial and earthly forces for strange effects. The sixth is al-niranjat, mixing earthly substances for strange phenomena. The seventh is alchemy, aimed at transmuting metals into gold and silver. Ghazali is merely describing these disciplines; he does not endorse their validity at this point.

أحكام النجوم
الفراسة
التعبير

النيرنجات
الكيمياء

Correction: Ghazali is only describing what the philosophers claim; he does not endorse the validity of each science. In fact, he later critiques many of them, especially astrology and alchemy.

Ghazali's Precise Objection
Ghazali states he does not reject the soul as a substance but challenges the rational proofs as sufficient.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's precise objection to the philosophers: he does not deny the soul's substantiality, but denies that reason alone can prove it without revelation.

Core Idea
Ghazali challenges the sufficiency of rational proofs, not the soul's substance itself.

Imam Ghazali has just summarized the philosophers' detailed account of the soul's powers. He now clarifies his own position. His objection is not against the idea of the soul as a self-subsistent substance—which he admits may be true and even supported by revelation—but against the claim that reason alone can prove it. This sets the stage for him to demand their proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the careful distinctions Ghazali makes.
Pay attention to his use of 'لا نعترض' and 'ننكر'.
2
Identify what Ghazali accepts
Note that Ghazali says nothing they mentioned must be rejected in the Sharia, and he does not object as one who thinks the soul's substance is impossible for God or opposite to revelation.
He even says the Sharia may confirm it in the discussion of resurrection.
3
Identify what Ghazali rejects
He denies the claim that mere reason indicates the soul's substantiality and that the philosophers are independent of revelation.
His objection is methodological, not doctrinal.
4
Understand the demand for proof
Ghazali says 'let us demand from them the proofs.' The burden is on the philosophers to produce rational proofs.
This frames the rest of the discussion.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why Ghazali makes this distinction. He shows respect for revealed truth while critiquing overreliance on reason.
This is key to understanding his approach in the Tahafut.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذا إيجاز ما فصلوه من القوى الحيوانية ، والإنسانية ، وطولوا بذكرها ، مع الإعراض عن ذكر القوى النباتية ، إذ لا حاجة إلى ذكرها في غرضنا . وليس شيء مما ذكروه ، مما يجب إنكاره في الشرع ، فإنها أمور مشاهدة ، أجرى الله تعالى العادة بها . وإنما نريد أن نعترض الآن على دعواهم ، معرفة كون النفس جوهراً قائماً بنفسه ، ببراهين العقل ، ولسنا نعترض على دعواهم ، اعتراض من يبعد ذلك من قدرة الله تعالى ، أو يرى أن الشرع جاء بنقيضه ، بل ربما نبين في تفصيل الحشر والنشر ، أن الشرع مصدق له ، ولكنا ننكر دعواهم ، دلالة مجرد العقل عليه ، والاستغناء عن الشرع فيه . فلنطالبهم بالأدلة ، ولهم فيها براهين كثيرة بزعمهم .
This is a summary of what they detailed regarding the animal and human powers, and they were lengthy in their mention, while omitting mention of the vegetative powers, as there is no need to mention them for our purpose. And nothing they have mentioned must be rejected in the revealed law, for they are observable matters which God Almighty has made habitual. But we intend now to object to their claim that they know the soul to be a self-subsistent substance (jawhar qā'im bi-nafsihī) by rational proofs. We do not object to their claim as one who considers that impossible for God's power, or who holds that the revealed law came with its opposite. Rather, we may show in the detailed discussion of resurrection and gathering that the revealed law confirms it. But we deny their claim that mere reason indicates it, and that they are independent of revelation therein. So let us demand from them the proofs, and they have many proofs in their claim.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage clarifies Ghazali's approach. He says the foregoing description of soul powers is unobjectionable in Sharia. His intended objection is not against the philosophers' claim that the soul is a self-subsistent substance per se—he acknowledges it may be true and even supported by revelation regarding resurrection—but he denies that purely rational proofs alone can establish it. He distinguishes between rejecting the claim as false (which he does not do) and rejecting the claim that reason alone suffices (which he does). He then calls for the philosophers to present their proofs.

براهين العقل
الشرع
الحشر والنشر

Correction: Ghazali only denies that reason alone can prove it, not the doctrine itself, which he admits may be confirmed by revelation.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's challenge is not to the soul's substance but to the claim that reason alone can prove it without revelation.

?

What is one specific belief or practice where you need to realign yourself with the Book and the Sunna, even if it feels less 'reasonable' to you?

Ghazali's Method: Attacking Rational Proof, Not the Doctrine
Ghazali clarifies his exact target: rational proofs for the soul as substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's precise methodological approach: he challenges only the rational proof for the soul as a self-subsistent substance, not the possibility of the doctrine itself.

Core Idea
Ghazali's target is rationalistic epistemology, not theological doctrine.

In this passage, Ghazali sets the stage for his critique of the philosophers. He clarifies that he is not objecting to the idea of the soul as a substance per se, nor is he arguing from religious texts. Instead, he focuses on the philosophers' claim that reason alone—without revelation—can prove this doctrine. This methodological move is crucial for understanding the entire Tahafut.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse. Then read the translation. Let Ghazali's own words set the stage.
Notice the clarity of his methodological distinction.
2
Identify what Ghazali is NOT attacking
Find the statements where Ghazali says he is not objecting to the philosophers' description of faculties, nor to the possibility that the soul is a substance by divine power or scripture.
He explicitly says 'we are not objecting' to those.
3
Identify what Ghazali IS attacking
Locate the exact claim he objects to: the philosophers' claim that bare reason proves the soul is a self-subsistent substance, independent of revelation.
This is the epistemological core of his attack.
4
Consider why this distinction matters
Reflect on why Ghazali limits his critique. How does this focus on rational proof shape the entire Tahafut?
It shows he is not a wholesale opponent of philosophy, but a critic of its rationalist pretensions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذا إيجاز ما فصلوه من القوى الحيوانية ، والإنسانية ، وطولوا بذكرها ، مع الإعراض عن ذكر القوى النباتية ، إذ لا حاجة إلى ذكرها في غرضنا . وليس شيء مما ذكروه ، مما يجب إنكاره في الشرع ، فإنها أمور مشاهدة ، أجرى الله تعالى العادة بها . وإنما نريد أن نعترض الآن على دعواهم ، معرفة كون النفس جوهراً قائماً بنفسه ، ببراهين العقل ، ولسنا نعترض على دعواهم ، اعتراض من يبعد ذلك من قدرة الله تعالى ، أو يرى أن الشرع جاء بنقيضه ، بل ربما نبين في تفصيل الحشر والنشر ، أن الشرع مصدق له ، ولكنا ننكر دعواهم ، دلالة مجرد العقل عليه ، والاستغناء عن الشرع فيه . فلنطالبهم بالأدلة ، ولهم فيها براهين كثيرة بزعمهم .
This is a summary of what they detailed about the animal and human faculties, and they lengthened mentioning them, while refraining from mentioning the vegetative faculties, since there is no need to mention them in our purpose. And nothing of what they mentioned is necessarily to be denied in the Law, for they are observable matters that Allah Almighty has made customary. But we now want to object to their claim of knowing the soul as a self-subsistent substance by rational proofs, and we are not objecting to their claim with the objection of one who considers that impossible for the power of Allah Almighty, or thinks that the Law has come with its opposite. Rather, perhaps we will clarify in the detailed discussion of resurrection and dispersal that the Law affirms it, but we deny their claim that bare reason indicates it and that there is no need for the Law regarding it. So let us demand from them the proofs, and they have, in their claim, many proofs.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage is a methodological preface. Ghazali first notes that the philosophers' lengthy discussion of animal and human faculties (ignoring vegetative ones) is not objectionable from a religious perspective, as these are observable phenomena that Allah has made customary. He then clarifies the precise target of his critique: he is not denying the possibility that the soul is a self-subsistent substance per se—he acknowledges that Islamic teachings on resurrection may actually support it. Instead, he specifically attacks the philosophers' claim that purely rational proofs, independent of revelation, can demonstrate that the soul is a self-subsistent substance. Thus, his objection is epistemological, not substantive. He concludes by stating he will demand their arguments, noting they claim to have many proofs.

Refuting the Third Proof on the Locus of Knowledge
Ghazali presents and refutes the philosophers' third proof about the locus of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali refutes the philosophers' argument that knowledge cannot be in a part of the body by using the concept of metaphorical language (tajwuz).

Core Idea
Attributing knowledge to the whole person is metaphorical, like saying a person is 'seeing' when sight is in the eyes.

The philosophers had a third proof that attempted to show that knowledge cannot be located in a physical part of the body. They argued that since we call the whole person 'knowing', knowledge must be in the whole, not a part. Ghazali immediately labels this argument as nonsense and provides a clear refutation based on our everyday metaphorical language.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' proof
Read the first part of the passage where the philosophers claim that if knowledge were in a part, only that part would be the knower.
Notice how they conclude that knowledge must then be in the whole person.
2
Identify Ghazali's rebuttal
Ghazali calls this 'nonsense' and gives examples: we call a person 'seeing' and 'hearing' even though these faculties are in specific organs.
This shows that the attribution to the whole is metaphorical.
3
Analyze the Baghdad analogy
Ghazali compares this to saying someone is 'in Baghdad' when they are only in a part of it. Understand how this clarifies the metaphorical usage.
Analogies help illustrate logical fallacies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قولهم : إن العلم لو كان فى جزء من الجسم ، لكان العالم ذلك الجزء ، دون سائر أجزاء الإنسان ، والإنسان يقال له عالم ، والعالمية صفة له على الجملة ، من غير نسبة إلى محل مخصوص وهذا هوس ، فإنه يسمى ، مبصراً وسامعاً ، وذائقاً ، وكذا البهيمة توصف به ، وذلك لا يدل ، على أن إدراك المحسوسات ليس بالجسم ؛ بل هو نوع من التجوز ، كما يقال : فلان فى بغداد وإن كان هو في جزء من جملة بغداد لا فى جميعها ، ولكن يضاف إلى الجملة
Their statement: If knowledge were in a part of the body, then that part would be the knower, to the exclusion of the other parts of the human being. Yet the human being is called 'knower' (ʿālim), and the property of being a knower belongs to him as a whole, without relation to a specific locus. This is nonsense, for he is called 'seeing' (mubṣir), 'hearing' (sāmiʿ), and 'tasting' (dhāʾiq), and likewise an animal is described as such. That does not indicate that the perception of sensibles is not by the body; rather it is a kind of metaphorical usage (tajwuz), just as it is said: 'So-and-so is in Baghdad' even though he is in a part of Baghdad, not in all of it, but the attribution is to the whole.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' third proof and Ghazali's refutation. The proof argues: if knowledge were located in a specific part of the body, then only that part would be the knower, yet we say the whole person is knowing. Ghazali responds by calling this 'nonsense' (hawas). He points out that we also say a person is 'seeing' or 'hearing', even though sight is in the eyes and hearing in the ears. This does not prove that perception is not bodily; it is simply a metaphorical way of speaking (tajwuz), just as we say someone is in Baghdad even though he is only in one part of it. Thus the attribution of knowledge to the whole person does not negate that the actual locus of knowledge is a part.

Key Terms
العالمية
تجوز
مبصر
سامع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If we say 'Zayd is knowing', then knowledge must be in all of Zayd, not in a part.
Correction: Ghazali explains that this is metaphorical language, like calling a person 'seeing' even though sight is only in the eyes.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why is the philosophers' argument that 'if knowledge were in a part, only that part would be the knower' invalid?
A) Because knowledge is immaterial and cannot be in any part.
B) Because we use metaphorical language when attributing perceptual abilities to the whole person, as with sight and hearing.
C) Because the philosophers are ignorant of anatomy.
D) Because the whole heart is the locus of knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali shows that attributing knowledge to the whole person is a metaphorical usage, not proof that knowledge is not located in a part.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you proudly say 'I know the truth', are you aware that your knowledge is limited to a part of your being? How does this recognition humble your claim of certainty and open you to learning more?
?
Think of a quality you attribute to yourself, like 'I am a good listener'. Is that true of your whole self, or only a part? Do you use such attributions to deceive yourself about your actual capacity, avoiding the hard work of improving that part?
?
The philosophers assumed that whole-person attribution disproves part-specific locus. Where in your life do you make similar categorical mistakes, assuming that because something is said of the whole, it must be true of the whole equally? Commit to identifying one such assumption today and question it.

The Ignorant Soul's Pain and Distraction
Ghazali describes the ignorant soul's pain and distraction using the numbness analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the ignorant soul suffers loss of pleasure without feeling it, and how bodily preoccupations act as a numbness like in the experience of a fearful or numb person.

Core Idea
Bodily distractions numb the soul to its own true pain.

Ghazali begins by diagnosing the condition of the ignorant soul in this worldly life. He argues that although the soul deserves to suffer from losing its proper pleasure, the constant engagement with the body makes it forget itself and its pain. This section uses a powerful analogy of a numb limb exposed to fire to show how the suffering will eventually be felt—all at once—when the bodily distractions fall away.

Guided Path
1
Read Ghazali's opening claim
Read the first sentence: the ignorant soul deserves to suffer pain from the loss of soul's pleasure. Notice the word 'حقها' (its right) — this is a moral and metaphysical claim, not merely descriptive.
The soul has a right to pleasure; losing it is a real deprivation.
2
Understand the distraction mechanism
Ghazali says preoccupation with the body makes the soul forget itself and distracts it from its pain. Reflect on how daily concerns can make one unaware of deeper spiritual malaise.
This is analogous to a person in fear not feeling other pains.
3
Analyze the numbness analogy
Read the analogy of the numb person exposed to fire. The numbness prevents feeling the fire's pain, but when numbness ends, the pain is immense and sudden. This illustrates how the ignorant soul will eventually feel its loss after death or when bodily occupations cease.
Notice the double analogy: al-kha'if (the fearful) and al-khadir (the numb). The key point is the sudden onset of pain.
4
Identify the lesson's core conclusion
The passage ends with 'دفعة واحدة هجوماً' — all at once, attacking. This sets up the idea that the soul's suffering is deferred but inevitable. Consider how this contrasts with the wise person who seeks real pleasure now.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والنفس الجاهلة فى الحياة الدنيا ، حقها أن تتألم بفوات لذة النفس ، ولكن الاشتغال بالبدن ، ينسيه نفسه ، ويلهيه عن ألمه ؛ كالخائف لا يحس بالألم وكالخدر لا يحس بالنار ، فإذا بقيت ناقصة ، حتى انحط عنها شغل البدن ، كان في صورة الخدر ، إذا عرض على النار ، فلا يحس بالألم ، فإذا زال الخدر شعر بالألم العظيم دفعة واحدة هجوماً . والنفوس المدركة للمعقولات ، قد تلتذ بها التذاذاً خفياً، قاصراً عما تقتضيه طباعها ، وذلك أيضاً لشواغل البدن وأنس النفس بشهواتها . ومثاله مثال المريض ، الذى فى فيه مرارة ، يستبشع الشيء الطيب الحلو و يستهجن الغذاء ، الذى هو أتم أسباب اللذة فى حقه ، فلا يتلذذ به لما عرض له من المرض . فالنفوس الكاملة بالعلوم ، إذا انحطت عنها أعباء البدن وشواغله بالموت ، كان مثاله مثال من عرض عليه الطعم الألذ ، والذوق الأطيب ، وكان به عارض من مرض ، يمنعه من الإدراك ، فزال العارض ، فأدرك اللذة العظيمة دفعة . من أو مثال اشتد عشقه في حق شخص ، فضاجعه ذلك الشخص وهو نائم ، أو مغمى عليه ، أو سكران ، لا يحس به ، فيتنبه فجأة ، فيشعر بلذة الوصال ، بعد طول الانتظار ، دفعة واحدة وهذه اللذات حقيرة ، بالإضافة إلى اللذات الروحانية العقلية ، إلا أنه لا يمكن تفهيمها للإنسان ، إلا بأمثلة مما شاهده الناس في هذه الحياة وهذا كما أنا لو أردنا أن نفهم الصبى أو العنين لذة الجماع ، لم نقدر عليه ، إلا بأن نمثله ، في حق الصبي باللعب ، الذي هو ألذ الأشياء عنده ؛ وفى حق العنين ، بلذة الأكل الطيب ، مع شدة الجوع ، ليصدق بأصل وجود اللذة ، ثم يعلم أن ما فهمه بالمثال ، ليس يحقق عنده لذة الجماع ، وأن ذلك لا يدرك إلا بالذوق
The ignorant soul in this worldly life—its right is to suffer pain from the loss of the soul's pleasure, but preoccupation with the body makes it forget itself and distracts it from its pain; like one who is afraid does not feel pain, and like one who is numb does not feel fire. So when it remains deficient until the occupation of the body falls away from it, it is in the form of the numb person when exposed to fire: he does not feel pain, but when the numbness ceases he feels immense pain all at once, attacking him. And the souls that apprehend intelligibles (al-maʿqūlāt) may take pleasure in them with a hidden pleasure, deficient relative to what their natures require, and that too is due to the preoccupations of the body and the soul's familiarity with its desires. Its example is the example of a sick person in whose mouth there is bitterness; he finds repugnant the pleasant sweet thing and deems loathsome the nourishment that is the most complete cause of pleasure in his case, so he does not delight in it because of the illness that befell him. So the souls perfected by knowledge, when the burdens of the body and its preoccupations fall away from them through death, their example is the example of one to whom the most delicious taste and the most pleasant flavor are presented, but there was an obstacle of illness preventing perception; then the obstacle ceases, and he perceives immense pleasure all at once. Or the example of one whose passion for a person has intensified, and that person lies with him while he is asleep, or unconscious, or drunk, not perceiving him; then he suddenly awakens and feels the pleasure of union after long waiting, all at once. And these pleasures are insignificant compared to the spiritual intellectual pleasures (al-ladhdhāt al-rūḥāniyya al-ʿaqliyya). However, it is not possible to make them understood to a person except by examples of what people have witnessed in this life. This is like if we wanted to make a boy or a eunuch understand the pleasure of intercourse: we cannot do so except by giving an example—for the boy, by play, which is the most pleasant thing to him; and for the eunuch, by the pleasure of delicious food when extremely hungry—so that he may affirm the existence of the pleasure in principle, then he knows that what he understood by the example does not truly realize for him the pleasure of intercourse, and that it cannot be grasped except by direct experience (al-dhawq).
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage describes the condition of the ignorant soul and uses analogies to illustrate the superiority of spiritual pleasures. Ghazali begins by stating that the ignorant soul in this life deserves to suffer pain from losing the soul's true pleasure, but bodily preoccupations make it forget and distract it from that pain—like a fearful person who does not feel other pains, or a numb limb that does not feel fire until the numbness leaves, at which point the pain is immense. Similarly, souls that apprehend intelligibles experience a hidden pleasure, but it is limited due to bodily distractions and the soul's attachment to desires. To clarify, Ghazali gives the example of a sick person with a bitter mouth who finds even the sweetest food repulsive; thus he cannot enjoy it until his illness is removed. So the souls perfected by knowledge, when freed from the body by death, are like that sick person who recovers and suddenly tastes immense pleasure. Another example is a passionate lover whose beloved lies with him while he is asleep or unconscious; when he awakens, he feels the pleasure of union all at once. Ghazali stresses that these earthly pleasures are insignificant compared to spiritual intellectual pleasures, but these higher pleasures can only be made understandable through examples from this life. He compares this to explaining sexual pleasure to a boy or a eunuch: one must use analogies (play for the boy, delicious food for the eunuch) so they can affirm that such pleasure exists, even though the example does not convey the actual experience—which can only be known through direct experience (dhawq). This section sets the stage for the proofs that follow by establishing the limitations of earthly analogies and the superiority of intellectual pleasures.

Key Terms
النفس الجاهلة
البدن
الخدر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, why does the ignorant soul not feel its pain in this life?
A) Because the soul is purely good and cannot feel pain.
B) Because bodily preoccupations make it forget itself and distract it.
C) Because pain only exists in the afterlife.
D) Because the soul is not real.

Key Takeaway
Bodily distractions act as a numbness that delays the soul's experience of its true deprivation, which will be felt intensely when those distractions cease.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What bodily distractions are numbing you right now to the true pain of your soul's distance from Allah? Will you wait until the numbness of this world is violently ripped away at death, or will you seek to feel that pain now and turn back?
?
If you knew that your current pleasures are merely an anesthetic delaying an immense pain, would you continue as you are? What would you do to remove the numbness before it's too late?
?
Are you content with the temporary numbness of worldly distractions, or are you willing to face the painful truth about your soul's state? What step will you take today to stop being the 'ignorant soul' described by Ghazali?

Analogies for Spiritual Pleasures and the Need for Direct Experience
Ghazali uses analogies to explain spiritual pleasures and stresses the need for direct experience.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses analogies from this world to point to spiritual intellectual pleasures, and why such pleasures can only be truly known through direct experience (dhawq).

Core Idea
Spiritual pleasures cannot be described; they can only be hinted at through analogies and known by taste.

Ghazali now turns to the souls that do apprehend intelligibles (al-ma'qulat) but experience only a limited pleasure due to bodily distractions. He provides two analogies—the sick person with a bitter mouth and the sleeping lover—to illustrate how the perfected soul, freed from the body at death, will experience immense pleasure. He then explicitly states that these worldly analogies are insignificant compared to the spiritual pleasures, and that such higher pleasures can only be made understandable through examples. The passage culminates in the comparison to explaining sexual pleasure to a boy or a eunuch, emphasizing that only direct experience (dhawq) reveals the true nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the description of the knowing souls
Read the sentence about souls that apprehend intelligibles (المعقولات). Ghazali says they have a hidden pleasure, but deficient due to bodily distractions and attachment to desires. Note the contrast with the ignorant soul from the previous lesson.
These souls are aware of higher realities but unable to fully enjoy them.
2
Study the sick person analogy
The analogy of a sick person with a bitter mouth who finds sweet food repulsive. The illness prevents enjoyment. Similarly, bodily attachments prevent full delight in intelligibles.
The 'illness' is the soul's familiarity with bodily desires.
3
Examine the perfected souls at death
Ghazali says that when the perfected souls are freed from the body's burdens by death, they are like the sick person who recovers and suddenly tastes immense pleasure. Then he gives the lover analogy: a passionate person whose beloved lies with him while he is unconscious, then awakens to sudden pleasure.
Both analogies emphasize the suddenness (دفعة) of the pleasure after the obstacle is removed.
4
Understand the limitation of analogies
Ghazali explicitly states that these earthly pleasures are insignificant compared to spiritual intellectual pleasures. He then says they cannot be understood except through examples from this life. Read the example of the boy and the eunuch.
The boy understands through play, the eunuch through food—but neither truly knows sexual pleasure.
5
Grasp the epistemological conclusion
The passage ends with 'لا يدرك إلا بالذوق' — it cannot be grasped except by direct experience. This is a key principle: some realities are beyond description and require personal taste (dhawq).
This anticipates Sufi epistemology.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

According to Ghazali, how can spiritual intellectual pleasures be made understandable to humans?
A) Through direct rational demonstration.
B) By giving examples from the afterlife.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
You have heard descriptions of Paradise and the vision of Allah, but do you realize these analogies are only hints? Are you satisfied with mere descriptions and intellectual assent, or are you actively striving for the direct taste (dhawq) of spiritual realities through sincere worship and purification? When will you begin the journey?
?
The lover wakes to find the beloved present. Are you spiritually asleep or intoxicated with worldly desires, missing the presence of Allah that is always with you? What would it take for you to wake up right now? What single step will you take today to become more aware of His presence?

Moderation in Ethics: The Shari'ah's Prescription
Ghazali presents the Shari'ah's method of moderation in ethics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Shari'ah prescribes the middle path in ethics, using the examples of generosity and courage.

Core Idea
The Shari'ah's golden mean: neither excess nor deficiency.

In this passage, Ghazali explains that since human traits cannot be completely removed, the Shari'ah commands moderation between opposing extremes. He uses the analogy of lukewarm water and gives concrete examples from wealth and courage. Let's examine his argument and see how it applies to all ethical traits.

Guided Path
1
Understand the principle
Read how Ghazali introduces the idea that Shari'ah prescribes moderation because traits cannot be stripped.
Notice the phrase 'since it is not possible to strip these attributes'.
2
Reflect on the analogy
Consider the analogy of lukewarm water being neither hot nor cold—how does this help explain the middle path?
The analogy shows the middle is distant from both extremes.
3
Identify the examples
Find the two examples: generosity between miserliness and extravagance, and courage between cowardice and recklessness.
Each example has a clear middle and two extremes.
4
Generalize the principle
Note that Ghazali concludes 'and likewise in all ethics'—the same method applies to all traits.
This shows the universal applicability of moderation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما لم يمكن سلب هذه الصفات، فقد ورد الشرع في الأخلاق، بالتوسط بين كل طرفين متقابلين، لأن الماء الفاتر، لا حار ولا بارد، فكأنه بعيد من الصفتين، فلا ينبغي أن يبالغ في إمساك المال، فيستحكم فيه الحرص على المال ولا فى الإنفاق، فيكون مبذراً، ولا أن يكون ممتنعاً عن كل الأمور، فيكون جباناً، ولا منهمكا فى كل أمر، فيكون متهوراً، بل يطلب الجود، فإنه الوسط بين البخل والتبذير، والشجاعة، فإنها الوسط بين الجبن والتهور، وكذا في جميع الأخلاق .
And since it is not possible to strip these attributes, the Shari'ah has come in ethics with moderation between every two opposing extremes, because lukewarm water is neither hot nor cold, so it is as if far from both attributes. So one should not go to excess in holding onto wealth, so that greed for wealth becomes firmly established, nor in spending, so that one becomes wasteful; nor should one refrain from all matters, so that one becomes a coward, nor plunge into every matter, so that one becomes reckless. Rather, one should seek generosity, for it is the middle between miserliness and extravagance; and courage, for it is the middle between cowardice and recklessness. And likewise in all ethics.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage presents the Shari'ah's solution for the practical limitation that traits cannot be completely removed: it commands moderation (tawassut) between two opposing extremes. The author first states the principle: since complete removal is impossible, the Shari'ah comes with the way of moderation. He gives an analogy: lukewarm water is neither hot nor cold, so it is distant from both extremes, symbolizing the middle path. He then provides two examples with their extremes: in wealth, one should avoid both miserliness (excessive hoarding) and extravagance (excessive spending), and instead seek generosity (jud). In courage, one should avoid both cowardice (complete avoidance) and recklessness (heedless plunging), and instead seek courage (shaja'a). He concludes that this principle applies to all ethical traits.

Key Terms
التوسط (moderation)
الجود (generosity)
الشجاعة (courage)
البخل (miserliness)
التبذير (extravagance)
الجبن (cowardice)
التهور (recklessness)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Moderation means a numerical average or a middle position in all situations.
Correction: Ghazali's moderation is the mean relative to extremes, not a simple mathematical average; it requires discernment of the two extremes in each context.

Checkpoint
What method does the Shari'ah prescribe for ethics according to Ghazali?
A) Complete abandonment of all desires
B) Moderation (tawassut) between two opposing extremes
C) Following one's natural inclinations
D) Strict adherence to ascetic practices

Key Takeaway
The Shari'ah's ethical method is to find the middle path between two extremes, neither excess nor deficiency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own spending habits, do you lean towards miserliness or extravagance? What one concrete change can you make today to move toward generosity as defined by the Shari'ah?
?
Think of a recent situation where you felt fear. Did you retreat entirely (cowardice) or rush in recklessly? What would a courageous middle path have looked like, and are you willing to practice it next time?
?
Is there an area of your life where you have settled for indifference, thinking it is moderation? How can you, like the lukewarm water analogy, be distinctly positioned away from both extremes without losing intensity in virtue?

Ethical Refinement Through Shari'ah and the Two Virtues
Ghazali concludes that ethical refinement requires following Shari'ah, and true happiness combines knowledge and action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that true ethical refinement requires submission to the Shari'ah, and that ultimate happiness combines both knowledge and action.

Core Idea
Ethics without Shari'ah leads to self-destruction; happiness requires both knowledge and action.

Ghazali concludes his discussion on ethics by clarifying the essential role of the Shari'ah. He warns against following personal desire and presents the two virtues that lead to true happiness. This passage gives the final classification of people based on their ethical and spiritual state.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage silently. Then read the English translation to get the overall meaning.
Notice the key terms: Shari'ah, ethical refinement, virtues.
2
Identify the condition for ethical refinement
Find where Ghazali states the only way to refine ethics. What is the role of the Shari'ah in this process?
He says: 'there is no way to refine ethics except by observing the law of the Shari'ah'.
3
Note the warning about following desire
Ghazali warns that following personal desire makes one take his desire as a god. How does following the Shari'ah prevent this?
Following Shari'ah means acting and refraining at its signal, not by personal choice.
4
Understand the two virtues
Identify the two virtues Ghazali mentions: the virtue of knowledge (al-ilmiyya) and the virtue of action (al-amaliyya). Why are both needed?
5
Reflect on the three categories of people
Ghazali describes two extremes: the absolutely destroyed (lacks both virtues) and the absolutely happy (possesses both). What about those with only one virtue?
The passage implies an intermediate category, though not explicitly named.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلم الأخلاق طويل ، والشريعة بالغت في تفصيلها ، ولا سبيل إلى تهذيب الأخلاق ، إلا بمراعاة قانون الشرع ، فى العمل ، حتى لا يتبع الإنسان هواه ، فيكون قد اتخذ إلهه هواه ، بل يقلد الشرع ، فيقدم ويحجم بإشارته ، لا باختياره فتتهذب به أخلاقه . ومن عدم هذه الفضيلة في الخلق والعلم جميعاً ، فهو الهالك المطلق . ومن جمع الفضيلتين ، العلمية والعملية ، فهو العارف العابد ، وهو السعيد المطلق.
And the science of ethics is long, and the Shari'ah has elaborated in its details. And there is no way to refine ethics except by observing the law of the Shari'ah in action, so that a person does not follow his desire, for then he would have taken his desire as his god. Rather, he follows the Shari'ah, acting and refraining at its signal, not by his own choice, and thus his ethics become refined. And whoever lacks both virtues—ethical virtue and knowledge—is the absolutely destroyed one. And whoever combines the two virtues, the virtue of knowledge and the virtue of action, is the knower-worshipper, and he is the absolutely happy one.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage concludes the discussion on ethical refinement by emphasizing the role of the Shari'ah and distinguishing between three categories of people. The author first notes that the science of ethics is vast and the Shari'ah has detailed it extensively. The only way to refine ethics is to adhere to the law of the Shari'ah in action, so that one does not follow his own desire (which would make his desire his god). Instead, one should imitate the Shari'ah, acting and refraining at its signal, not by personal choice. This process refines his character. Then Ghazali gives a final classification: (1) One who lacks both ethical virtue (khuluq) and knowledge is the completely destroyed (halik mutlaq). (2) One who combines the two virtues—knowledge (al-ilmiyya) and action (al-amaliyya)—is the knower-worshipper (al-arif al-abid), and he is the absolutely happy (sa'id mutlaq).

Key Terms
تهذيب الأخلاق (ethical refinement)
الفضيلة العلمية (virtue of knowledge)
الفضيلة العملية (virtue of action)
العارف العابد (knower-worshipper)
السعيد المطلق (absolutely happy)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think ethical refinement is achieved by personal effort alone.
Correction: Ghazali stresses that refinement requires submission to the Shari'ah, not personal choice.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, who is the absolutely happy person?
A) One who possesses great knowledge
B) One who performs many acts of worship
C) One who combines both knowledge and action
D) One who follows his own desire with moderation

Key Takeaway
True happiness requires combining the virtue of knowledge and the virtue of action through submission to the Shari'ah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In which area of your life are you most tempted to follow your own desire instead of the signal of the Shari'ah? What specific step can you take today to realign that area with Allah's command?
?
Ghazali says that the one who lacks both virtue of knowledge and virtue of action is absolutely destroyed. Honestly, which of these two virtues is weaker in your life right now? What is one concrete action you will take this week to strengthen it?
?
You have taken your desire as your god whenever you prioritize it over the Shari'ah. Before you finish this reflection, identify one recurring desire you struggle with. Write down a clear, practical commitment to follow the Shari'ah in that matter starting now.

Moderation: The Middle Path in Islamic Ethics
Ghazali defines the Islamic principle of moderation between extremes in all moral virtues.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Islamic ethics prescribes a divinely guided moderation between extremes for every moral virtue.

Core Idea
Every virtue is a middle path between two blameworthy extremes.

Ghazali presents the foundational Islamic ethical principle of tawassut (moderation). He argues that since we cannot eliminate all attributes, the Revealed Law guides us to choose the balanced middle between every pair of opposing extremes. This passage lays out the method for identifying virtues like generosity and courage as the midpoint between their corresponding vices.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the literal translation. Notice the flow from principle to analogy to examples.
2
Identify the central analogy
Ghazali uses lukewarm water as an analogy for moderation. Ask yourself: How does this analogy illustrate the idea of being 'distant from both qualities'?
3
Trace the application to wealth
Find the two extremes related to wealth (miserliness and prodigality) and the virtue (generosity) that lies between them. Note how Ghazali warns against going to either extreme.
4
Trace the application to action
Similarly, find the two extremes of cowardice and recklessness, and the virtue of courage. Observe the parallel structure.
Ghazali then generalizes: 'And likewise in all moral virtues.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما لم يمكن سلب هذه الصفات، فقد ورد الشرع في الأخلاق، بالتوسط بين كل طرفين متقابلين، لأن الماء الفاتر، لا حار ولا بارد، فكأنه بعيد من الصفتين، فلا ينبغي أن يبالغ في إمساك المال، فيستحكم فيه الحرص على المال ولا فى الإنفاق، فيكون مبذراً، ولا أن يكون ممتنعاً عن كل الأمور، فيكون جباناً، ولا منهمكا فى كل أمر، فيكون متهوراً، بل يطلب الجود، فإنه الوسط بين البخل والتبذير، والشجاعة، فإنها الوسط بين الجبن والتهور، وكذا في جميع الأخلاق
And since it is not possible to strip these attributes, the Revealed Law (al-shar‘) has come in matters of character (al-akhlāq) with the principle of moderation (al-tawassuṭ) between every pair of opposing extremes, because lukewarm water is neither hot nor cold, so it is as if it is distant from both qualities. Thus one should not go to excess in hoarding wealth, so that miserliness (al-ḥirṣ) becomes entrenched in him, nor in spending, so that he becomes wasteful (mubadhdhir), nor should he refrain from all matters, so that he becomes cowardly (jabān), nor plunge into every matter, so that he becomes reckless (mutahawwir). Rather, he should seek generosity (al-jūd), for it is the middle between miserliness (al-bukhl) and prodigality (al-tabdhīr), and courage (al-shajā‘a), for it is the middle between cowardice (al-jubn) and recklessness (al-tahawwur). And likewise in all moral virtues (al-akhlāq).
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the Islamic ethical principle of moderation (tawassuṭ). Ghazali begins by stating that since it is impossible to completely strip the soul of bodily attributes, the Revealed Law (shar‘) has prescribed moderation between every pair of opposing extremes in moral character. He uses the analogy of lukewarm water: it is neither hot nor cold, thus distant from both extremes. He then applies this principle to specific virtues: in the matter of wealth, one should not be excessive in hoarding (which leads to miserliness) nor excessive in spending (which leads to wastefulness); rather, generosity (jūd) is the middle between miserliness (bukhl) and prodigality (tabdhīr). In the matter of action, one should not refrain from all matters (cowardice, jubn) nor plunge into every matter (recklessness, tahawwur); rather, courage (shajā‘a) is the middle between cowardice and recklessness. He concludes that this principle applies to all moral virtues.

Key Terms
شرع (Revealed Law)
أخلاق (moral character)
توسط (moderation)
طرفان متقابلان (two opposing extremes)
جود (generosity)
بخل (miserliness)
تبذير (prodigality)
شجاعة (courage)
جبن (cowardice)
تهور (recklessness)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The principle of moderation is a statistical average or compromise between two extremes.
Correction: It is a divinely defined middle path based on revelation (shar‘), not a human calculation; the middle is distinct from the extremes, as lukewarm water is neither hot nor cold.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the middle between miserliness and prodigality?
A) Frugality
B) Generosity
C) Wastefulness
D) Hoarding

Key Takeaway
Islamic ethics defines every virtue as the divinely revealed middle path between two blameworthy extremes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recent situation where you had to choose between holding back and overdoing it—whether in spending, speaking, or acting. Which extreme were you leaning toward? What would it have looked like to practice generosity or courage in that moment, and why did you not choose it? Commit now to one concrete step you will take tomorrow to move closer to the middle path in that area.
?
The lukewarm water analogy shows that the middle is not weak but distinct from both extremes. Is there a virtue you have avoided pursuing because you feared it would make you 'lukewarm' or indifferent? What is one specific fear or assumption that has kept you from actively seeking that virtue? Write it down, and then reframe it using Ghazali's principle that the middle path is a deliberate, praiseworthy state.
?
The passage says the Revealed Law commands moderation—not personal preference or cultural norm. Where in your life have you justified an extreme (like miserliness or recklessness) by calling it 'just the way I am' rather than seeing it as a deviation from the divine standard? What would it cost you to realign that area with the middle path, and are you willing to pay that price today?

The Sharia as the Sole Path to Moral Refinement
Ghazali states the exclusive role of Sharia in moral refinement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali insists that moral refinement can only be achieved by following the Sharia, not personal desire.

Core Idea
Moral refinement requires submitting to Sharia, not following your desires.

Ghazali makes a bold claim: the science of character is vast, but the Sharia has already covered it in detail. He argues that the only way to truly refine your character is by observing the rule of the Revealed Law in your actions. This short but profound passage challenges those who think reason alone can perfect the self.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the key terms: ʿilm al-akhlāq, tahdhīb al-akhlāq, qānūn al-sharʿ, hawā.
The passage is dense with meaning despite its brevity.
2
Identify the main thesis
What is the only way to refine character according to Ghazali? Find the phrase 'لا سبيل إلى تهذيب الأخلاق إلا بمراعاة قانون الشرع'.
This is the core claim.
3
Understand the contrast
Ghazali contrasts following the Sharia with following one's desire (hawā). Why does he equate following desire with taking it as a god? Reflect on the Quranic reference.
This shows the gravity of the issue.
4
Apply the teaching
Consider how this principle applies to your own life. What does it mean to 'advance and retreat by its signal'?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلم الأخلاق طويل، والشريعة بالغت في تفصيلها، ولا سبيل إلى تهذيب الأخلاق، إلا بمراعاة قانون الشرع، فى العمل، حتى لا يتبع الإنسان هواه، فيكون قد اتخذ إلهه هواه، بل يقلد الشرع، فيقدم ويحجم بإشارته، لا باختياره فتتهذب به أخلاقه
And the science of character (ʿilm al-akhlāq) is extensive, and the Revealed Law (al-sharīʿa) has been exhaustive in its elaboration. There is no way to refine character (tahdhīb al-akhlāq) except by observing the rule of the Revealed Law (qānūn al-sharʿ) in action, so that a person does not follow his own desire (hawā), thereby taking his desire as his god. Rather, he should imitate the Revealed Law (yuqallid al-sharʿ), advancing and retreating by its signal, not by his own choice, and thus his character is refined through it.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage is a methodological statement on moral refinement. Ghazali asserts that the science of ethics (ʿilm al-akhlāq) is extensive, and the Sharia has already provided a detailed elaboration of it. The only way to refine one's character (tahdhīb al-akhlāq) is to observe the law of the Sharia in one's actions. The goal is to prevent the person from following his own desire (hawā), which would mean taking his desire as his god (a reference to the Quranic concept of 'those who take their desires as their god'). Instead, one should imitate (yuqallid) the Sharia, meaning he should advance and retreat only by its command, not by his own choice. Through this submission, his character becomes refined.

Key Terms
علم الأخلاق (science of character)
شريعة (Revealed Law)
تهذيب الأخلاق (refinement of character)
هوى (desire/whim)
تقليد (imitation/compliance)
قانون الشرع (rule of the Revealed Law)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think moral refinement can be achieved through reason alone without divine guidance.
Correction: Ghazali explicitly states that refinement is impossible except by observing the rule of the Sharia.

Checkpoint

A) Following one's innate reason
B) Observing the rule of the Sharia in action

D) Pursuing philosophical contemplation

Key Takeaway
Moral refinement is only possible through submission to the Sharia, not by following personal desire.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
If character refinement is impossible without imitating the Sharia, what does that mean for your current spiritual practices? Are you truly following the Sharia's signals, or are you selectively applying them to fit your lifestyle? Will you commit to changing one specific habit this week to align with the Sharia, regardless of personal preference?

The Afterlife: Known Through Sharia, Not Reason Alone
Ghazali distinguishes agreement on doctrine from disagreement on method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's argument that the afterlife and the soul's survival are known through revelation, not through unaided reason.

Core Idea
Ghazali accepts the conclusions but rejects the method: reason alone cannot know the afterlife.

In this passage, Ghazali clarifies that his disagreement with the philosophers is not about the reality of the afterlife or the soul's survival—Muslims affirm these. Instead, he objects to their claim that such matters can be known by mere reason without revelation. This lesson unpacks that crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the English translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Ghazali starts by affirming agreement.
2
Identify what Ghazali does not deny
Ghazali explicitly states two things he does not deny. Find them in the text and note them.
These are points of agreement with the philosophers.
3
Identify the real point of objection
Ghazali says 'We only object to them on account of their claim to know that by reason alone.' This is the core of his critique. Ask yourself why method matters so much.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونحن نقول: أكثر هذه الأمور ليست على مخالفة الشرع، فإنا لا ننكر أن في الآخرة أنواعاً اللذات، أعظم من المحسوسات، ولا ننكر بقاء النفس عند مفارقة البدن، ولكنا عرفنا ذلك بالشرع، إذ قد ورد بالمعاد، ولا يفهم المعاد إلا ببقاء النفس؛ وإنما أنكرنا عليهم، من قبل دعواهم معرفة ذلك بمجرد العقل
And we say: Most of these matters are not contrary to the Revealed Law (shar‘), for we do not deny that in the Hereafter there are types of pleasures greater than sensory ones, and we do not deny the survival of the soul upon separation from the body, but we know that through the Revealed Law, since the doctrine of the return (al-ma‘ād) has been transmitted, and the return is not understood except through the survival of the soul. We only object to them on account of their claim to know that by reason alone (bi-mujarrad al-‘aql).
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage is Ghazali's response to the philosophers' doctrines about the afterlife. He begins by stating that most of these matters (i.e., the philosophers' claims about the soul's survival and the reality of intellectual pleasures) are not contrary to the Sharia, because Muslims also affirm that there are pleasures in the Hereafter greater than sensory ones and that the soul survives after death. However, Ghazali emphasizes that Muslims know these truths through the Sharia, since the doctrine of the return (al-ma‘ād) has been transmitted in revelation, and the return can only be understood through the survival of the soul. The real point of disagreement is the philosophers' claim to know these matters by reason alone (bi-mujarrad al-‘aql). Thus, Ghazali does not deny the philosophers' conclusions per se, but denies their epistemological method of relying solely on reason without revelation.

Key Terms
آخرة (Hereafter)
لذات (pleasures)
محسوسات (sensory things)
بقاء النفس (survival of the soul)
شرع (Revealed Law)
معاد (return/resurrection)
مجرد العقل (mere reason)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali denies the survival of the soul or the afterlife.
Correction: Ghazali affirms both but insists they are known through revelation, not reason alone.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of Ghazali's objection to the philosophers' view on the afterlife?
A) They deny the afterlife entirely
B) They claim to know the afterlife through reason alone, without revelation
C) They reject the survival of the soul
D) They affirm the physical resurrection

Key Takeaway
The afterlife is a matter of revelation (shari'a), not a conclusion of unaided reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about Paradise, do you rely more on descriptions you've read in the Quran or on rational imagination? If a rational objection arose against a revealed description, would you trust your reason or the text?
?
In your daily life, do you submit to the shari’a even when your mind cannot fully comprehend its wisdom? Where is the biggest gap between your intellectual satisfaction and your submission to Allah's command?
?
Can you point to one belief about the afterlife that you hold solely because Allah informed you, not because it makes philosophical sense to you? If not, what does that say about your reliance on revelation vs. reason?
